Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Types of Rukhsah: Real and Metaphorical
Classifies rukhsah into real and metaphorical types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and metaphorical types of rukhsah and apply the Hanafi criteria to classify any given dispensation.

Core Idea
A true license retains the original ruling; if the ruling itself is removed, the license is only metaphorical.

In this passage, the author unpacks the Hanafi classification of rukhsah (license) into four types, ranking them by how closely they adhere to the literal meaning of 'dispensation'. Understanding this taxonomy is crucial because it determines whether a ruling's underlying prohibition remains intact or is fully lifted. Let's walk through the criteria that separate a real license from a metaphorical one.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two broad categories of rukhsah
Understand that rukhsah is divided into real (haqiqi) and metaphorical (majazi). Real rukhsah keeps the original ruling intact but changes its description (e.g., a prohibition becomes permissible for an excused person). Metaphorical rukhsah removes the original ruling entirely, either temporarily or permanently.
The passage focuses on the last two (metaphorical) and mentions the first (highest real) as a benchmark.
2
Identify the third type: naskh (abrogation)
Naskh completely removes the original ruling and replaces it permanently. Since the essence of the original ruling falls, calling it rukhsah is metaphorical. It is the furthest from the real meaning of rukhsah.
Example: abrogation of the earlier qibla direction.
3
Identify the fourth type: rukhsat isqat (license of removal)
Here, the prohibition falls only for the excused person (e.g., eating carrion for the compelled), but the prohibition remains in general for others. The cause (necessity) remains prescribed, so this type is closer to real rukhsah than naskh, yet still metaphorical because the ruling is removed for the individual.
This is called 'rukhsat isqat' or 'license of dropping'.
4
Understand why the first type is the highest real rukhsah
In the first type, both the original ruling and its cause remain, yet the act is permitted without reproach. This is the highest degree because the dispensation is a pure alleviation while the legal structure stays intact.
The passage contrasts this with the second type where the ruling is delayed, making the license less complete.
5
Apply the Hanafi rule for classifying any rukhsah
Whenever you encounter a shari' dispensation, ask: Does the original ruling (e.g., prohibition) remain in principle? If yes, it is real rukhsah. If the ruling itself is removed (even for limited cases), it is metaphorical rukhsah.
The classification affects how we understand the permanence and scope of the license.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالرخصة مجاز، والثالث أتم في المجازية، كالأول في حقيقته والرابع: ماسقط (عنا حرمته مع وجود العذر لأجل وجود العذر ) و مع مشروعيته (بقاء حرمته) في الجملة ، ويسمى هذا رخصة إسقاط، كسقوط حرمة الميتة للمضطر فأكل الميتة جائز للمضطر، وإن كانت الحرمة باقية و مشروعة في وقت عدم العذر، وهذا معنى قوله : " في الجملة ، ولكن سقطت تلك الحرمة في حق المضطر الاضطراره وحاجته الشديدة، ولوسقطت الحرمة كاملة، وبالنسبة إلى كل أحد لم تبق الميتة حراماً. قالوا (علماء الأصول ): تسميه الأخيرين النسخ ورخصة الإسقاط) بالرخصة مجاز؛ لأن تغيير الحكم من عسر إلى يسر يقتضي بحسب الحقيقة بقاء أصل الحكم وتغيير صفته ، وفي هذين القسمين قدسقط ذات الحكم ، فهو رخصة مجازاً)، والثالث (أعنى النسخ) أتم في المجازية (في كونه رخصة مجازاً؛ لسقوط ذات الحكم ، فيكون بعيداً عن المعنى الحقيقى للرخصة غاية البعد ، بخلاف الرابع ، فإن السبب فيه بقى مشروعاً في الجملة أي) في محلّ عدم وجود العذر) كالأول فإنه أتم في الحقيقة من النوع الثاني، أي في كونه رخصة حقيقية؛ لأن حكم الأصل مع سببه (المحرم) لما كان باقياً في الأول ، ومع ذلك كان الإقدام على الفعل مشروعاً من غير مؤاخذة كان الأول في أعلى درجات الرُّخص، بخلاف الثاني فإن السبب فيه وإن كان قائماً، لكن الحكم إذا تراخى كانت العزيمة ناقصة، فكذلك الرخصة المقابلة لها.
is a metaphor for rukhsah, and the third is more complete in metaphor, like the first in its real meaning. The fourth: that whose prohibition falls due to the existence of the excuse, with its sacredness remaining in general, and this is called rukhsat isqat, like the falling of the prohibition of carrion for the one compelled. Eating carrion is permissible for the one compelled, even though the prohibition remains and is prescribed in the time of no excuse. This is the meaning of 'in general'. But that prohibition fell for the compelled due to his dire necessity and severe need. If the prohibition had fallen completely and for everyone, carrion would not remain forbidden. The scholars of usul said: calling the last two (naskh and rukhsat isqat) a rukhsah is metaphorical, because changing a ruling from hardship to ease requires in reality the survival of the original ruling and a change in its description. In these two categories, the essence of the ruling has fallen, so it is a metaphorical rukhsah. The third (i.e., naskh) is more complete in metaphor (in being a metaphorical rukhsah) because the essence of the ruling has fallen, so it is extremely distant from the real meaning of rukhsah, unlike the fourth, because the reason in it remains prescribed in general, i.e., in the context of absence of excuse. Like the first, it is more complete in reality than the second type, i.e., in being a real rukhsah. Because the original ruling with its (prohibiting) cause remained in the first, yet proceeding with the act was prescribed without reproach. Thus the first is at the highest degree of licenses, unlike the second, for the reason in it exists, but if the ruling is delayed, the resolution ('azima) becomes deficient. Similarly, the license corresponding to it.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the types of rukhsah (license/dispensation) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author explains that the third type (naskh, abrogation) and the fourth type (rukhsat isqat, license of removal) are called rukhsah only metaphorically because in both the original ruling is entirely removed—in naskh permanently, in rukhsat isqat only for the excused person. In contrast, the first two types (not detailed here) involve a change in description while the original ruling remains, making them real (haqiqi) licenses. The author emphasizes that the first type is the highest degree of real rukhsah because the original ruling and its cause remain, yet the act is permitted. The fourth type is closer to real rukhsah than the third because the cause remains prescribed in general. This distinction is crucial for understanding how dispensations operate in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsah): A dispensation that permits an action which is otherwise prohibited due to hardship, while the original restriction remains in principle.
رخصة إسقاط (rukhsat isqat): A license where the prohibition is dropped entirely for the excused person, but remains in general; e.g., eating carrion for the one compelled.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation: the complete removal of a legal ruling by a later revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul text, why are the third and fourth types of rukhsah called 'metaphorical' (majaz)?
A) Because they involve a change in the description of the ruling while the original ruling remains.
B) Because the essence of the original ruling (hukm) is removed entirely, either permanently or for the excused person.
C) Because they are only applicable in cases of extreme necessity.
D) Because they are temporary dispensations that expire after a fixed period.

Key Takeaway
The classification of rukhsah hinges on whether the original ruling (hukm) remains intact; if it is removed (even for an excused person), the license is metaphorical.

Validity in Worship and Transactions: Rational or Legal?
Defining sihha in worship and transactions, rational vs. legal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between validity in worship (rational) and transactions (legal) and analyze the dispute between theologians and jurists.

Core Idea
Validity in worship is rational (compliance with command), but in transactions, it is legal (dependent on divine stipulation).

This passage defines the concept of validity (sihha) in Islamic law, focusing on whether it is a rational or legal judgment. The author distinguishes between acts of worship and transactions, presenting the views of theologians (mutakallimun) and jurists (fuqaha’) on the purpose of worship. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying validity conditions in different legal domains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central classification
The passage first divides acts into worship (ibadat) and transactions (muamalat). Note that the author states the ruling of validity in worship is rational, while for transactions it is legal by consensus.
This sets the framework for the entire discussion.
2
Define the purpose (ghaya) of the act
The purpose is the goal or benefit realized by the act. For worship, there are two definitions: compliance with the command (mutakallimun) and dropping the obligation of recompense (fuqaha’). For transactions, the purpose is the realization of legal effects.
The definition of purpose determines whether validity is rational or legal.
3
Analyze the dispute over rational vs. legal
The author engages with the view that validity is purely a legal ruling (hukm wad’i). He argues that even for transactions, the act of performing the contract as prescribed is knowable by reason after the law, though the effects are legal.
This is a nuanced Hanafi position.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider prayer with presumed purity that later turns out to be invalid. According to the passage, this act did not achieve its purpose (compliance), so it is invalid and requires recompense. This shows the rational element: reason judges that the command was not fulfilled.
The example illustrates the practical consequence of the definition.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in worship, validity hinges on rational compliance; in transactions, it hinges on fulfilling legal conditions. If either is absent, the act is invalid.
This distinction affects rulings in both areas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٥: مسألة عقلية الصحة وشرعيتها في العبادات الحكم بالصحة في العبادات عقلى؛ لأنها استتباع الغاية، وهي في العبادات عند المتكلمين موافقة الأمر، وإن وجب القضاء، كالصلاة بظن الطهارة، وعند الفقهاء كونه مسقطا لوجوب القضاء تحقيقاً أو تقديراً، كما في الأداء، وبعد ورود الأمر، يعرف ذلك بلا توقف، وقد ظن أنها من أحكام الوضع وقيل بمعنى الموافقة عقل، وبمعنى الإسقاط وضعى أقول الاسقاط فرع التمامية، وهو بالموافقة، وهو عقلى وقيل في المعاملات وضعى اتفاقاً، لأن ترتب الثمرات على العقود موقوف على التوقيف البتة أقول: جعل العقود أسباباً لا ريب أنه مع الوضع، لكن الصحة هو الاتيان بها كما جعلها، وذلك هو المناط لاستتباع الثمرة، وهو بعد الشرع يعرف بالعقل، فتأمل واعلم أوّلاً: أن الغاية عبارة عن مقصد الفعل وفائدته التي تحصل وتجد في نهاية الفعل، ثم ذلك الفعل على نوعين من العبادات، كالصلاة والصوم. ومن المعاملات كالبيع و الشراء. ثم اختلف العلماء أولاً في غاية العبادات أنها ماهي؟ وثانياً أن الحكم بصحة فعل المكلف عبادة كان أو معاملة عقلى أو شرعي ؟ فبناء على هذين التمهدين قال المصنف: الحكم بالصحة في العبادات عقلى ، لأنها أي الصحة استتباع الفعل واستلزامه الغاية وفائدة مشروعيته، ثم ذكر الاختلاف في غاية الفعل وقال : وهي غاية الفعل في العبادات عند المتكلمين موافقة الأمر أمر الشارع أي كون الفعل المأمور به موافقاً لأمر الشارع هي غاية العبادات عند المتكلمين وإن وجب القضاء في بعض الصور لعدم موافقة أمر الشارع وعدم ترتب الغاية في الحقيقة كصلاة من صلى بظن الظهارة ، ثم ظهر أنه كان محدثاً أو غير طاهر الثوب أو غير طاهر البدن ، فيجب عليه القضاء. والغاية عند الفقهاء كون الفعل مسقطاً لوجوب القضاء، كما في الأداء، تحقيقاً ، فيما كان له قضاء، كصلوات الخمس والصوم، أو تقديراً فيما لا يكون له قضاء ،
25: A rational issue: validity and its legal nature in acts of worship. The ruling of validity in acts of worship is rational, because it is the attainment of the purpose (ghaya). That purpose, according to the theologians (mutakallimun), is compliance with the command, even if recompense (qada') is required—like prayer with a presumption of purity (then found to be impure). According to the jurists (fuqaha'), it is the act being capable of dropping the obligation of recompense, either actually or constructively, as in performance. After the command is known, this is known without delay. And it was thought that it is among the rulings of the law. Some said: the meaning of compliance is rational, and the meaning of dropping (the obligation) is legal. I say: dropping is a branch of completeness, which is compliance, and it is rational. Some said: in transactions it is legal by consensus, because the realization of fruits from contracts is absolutely dependent on legal stipulation. I say: making contracts causes is undoubtedly legal, but validity is performing them as they have been made, and that is the criterion for attaining the fruit, and it is known by reason after the law. So reflect. Know first: that the 'ghaya' (purpose) is the objective of the act and its benefit which is realized at the end of the act. Then that act is of two types: acts of worship like prayer and fasting, and transactions like sale and purchase. Then scholars differed first about what the purpose of acts of worship is, and secondly whether the ruling of validity of the act of the accountable person—whether worship or transaction—is rational or legal. Based on these two premises, the author said: The ruling of validity in acts of worship is rational, because it (validity) is the act attaining and necessitating the purpose and benefit of its legislation. Then he mentioned the difference regarding the purpose of the act and said: The purpose of the act in acts of worship according to the theologians is compliance with the command of the Lawgiver—that the commanded act be in accordance with the Lawgiver's command—that is the purpose of acts of worship according to the theologians, even if recompense is required in some cases due to not complying with the Lawgiver's command and the purpose not being realized in reality—like the prayer of one who prayed with a presumption of purity, then it became clear that he was in a state of hadath or his garment or body was impure, so he must make it up. And the purpose according to the jurists is the act being capable of dropping the obligation of recompense, as in performance: actually in cases that have a recompense like the five prayers and fasting, or constructively in cases that have no recompense.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the concept of validity (صحة) in Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on the distinction between acts of worship (عبادات) and transactions (معاملات). The author begins by stating that the ruling of validity in worship is rational (عقلى), not legal (شرعى), meaning it is derived from reason after knowing the legal command. He explains that validity is the act's attainment of its purpose (غاية). Two definitions of purpose are given: (1) For theologians (متكلمين), it is compliance with the divine command (موافقة الأمر), even if the obligation to make up (قضاء) arises (e.g., praying with a presumption of purity that turns out to be false). (2) For jurists (فقهاء), it is the act's ability to drop the obligation of recompense (إسقاط وجوب القضاء), either actually (like performing the prayer that then does not need to be repeated) or constructively (like acts that have no recompense at all). The author then notes that some thought validity is a legal ruling, but he corrects this by explaining that the foundation is rational. For transactions, there is consensus that validity is legal (وضعى) because the legal effects (ثمرات) of contracts depend entirely on legal stipulations. However, he refines this by noting that making contracts causes is legal, but the actual judgment of validity (performing the contract as intended) is known by reason once the legal framework is given. This is a classic Hanafi usul discussion emphasizing the rational basis of validity in worship versus the purely legal basis in transactions.

Key Terms
صحة (ṣiḥḥa): Validity: the act's attainment of its purpose (ghaya) or ability to drop the obligation of recompense.
غاية (ghāya): The purpose or end of the act; for worship, it is compliance with command or dropping recompense; for transactions, realization of legal effects.
موافقة الأمر (muwāfaqat al-amr): Compliance with the divine command; the purpose of worship according to mutakallimun.
إسقاط وجوب القضاء (isqāṭ wujūb al-qaḍāʾ): The act's ability to drop the obligation of making up the act; the purpose of worship according to fuqaha'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between the definition of validity in worship according to the theologians (mutakallimun) and the jurists (fuqaha')?
A) Theologians say validity is legal; jurists say it is rational.
B) Theologians say validity is compliance with command; jurists say it is dropping the obligation of recompense.
C) Theologians say validity requires actual purity; jurists do not.
D) Theologians say validity is for transactions; jurists say it is for worship.

Key Takeaway
Validity in worship is fundamentally a rational judgment of compliance with the command, while in transactions it is a legal determination of meeting divine stipulations.

Purpose (Ghayah) of Acts and Definition of Sahih
Defining sahih and its classification as rational or positive in usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two definitions of the ultimate purpose (ghayah) of acts of worship according to the mutakallimin and the Hanafi fuqaha, and identify the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
For Hanafi fuqaha, the purpose of worship is dropping the obligation of qada, not mere conformity to command.

Validity (sahih) is a core concept in usul al-fiqh, but what does it mean for an act to be valid? The scholars have differed on the ultimate purpose (ghayah) of acts of worship, which in turn shapes the definition of sahih. This lesson walks through the two major views: that of the theologians (mutakallimin) and that of the Hanafi jurists (fuqaha), clarifying the practical implications and the Hanafi reliance on the dropping of qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of ghayah
Locate in the passage the two definitions of the ultimate purpose (ghayah) of acts of worship: for the mutakallimin it is conformity to the command, even if qada is later required; for the fuqaha it is dropping the obligation of qada, actually or presumptively.
The passage explicitly contrasts these two views.
2
Understand the example of prayer with presumption of purity
The author gives the example of a person who prays presuming they are pure but later discovers impurity. According to the mutakallimin, the purpose (conformity) was not achieved, so qada is required. According to the fuqaha, the act was performed correctly with conditions and pillars, so it drops qada.
This illustrates the practical consequence of each definition.
3
Distinguish between acts with qada and those without
The fuqaha's definition includes two subcategories: actual dropping of qada (for acts like five daily prayers and fasting) and presumptive dropping (for acts with no qada, like Eid prayer).
Presumptive dropping means the act itself suffices and no replacement is needed.
4
Relate ghayah to the definition of sahih
Sahih is defined as the act's entailment of its ghayah. For the fuqaha, an act of worship is sahih if it drops the obligation of qada. This definition has epistemological implications discussed in the next lesson.
Understanding ghayah is essential to understanding sahih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبادات عند المتكلمين موافقة الأمر، وإن وجب القضاء، كالصلاة بظن الطهارة، وعند الفقهاء كونه مسقطا لوجوب القضاء تحقيقاً أو تقديراً، كما في الأداء، وبعد ورود الأمر، يعرف ذلك بلا توقف، وقد ظن أنها من أحكام الوضع وقيل بمعنى الموافقة عقل، وبمعنى الإسقاط وضعى اقول الاسقاط فرع التمامية، وهو بالموافقة، وهو عقلى وقيل في المعاملات وضعى اتفاقاً، لأن ترتب الثمرات على العقود موقوف على التوقيف البتة أقول: جعل العقود أسباباً لا ريب أنه مع الوضع، لكن الصحة هو الاتيان بها كما جعلها، وذلك هو المناط لاستتباع الثمرة، وهو بعد الشرع يعرف بالعقل، فتأمل واعلم أوّلاً: أن الغاية عبارة عن مقصد الفعل وفائدته التي تحصل وتجد في نهاية الفعل، ثم ذلك الفعل على نوعين من العبادات، كالصلاة والصوم. ومن المعاملات كالبيع و الشراء. ثم اختلف العلماء أولاً في غاية العبادات أنها ماهي؟ وثانياً أن الحكم بصحة فعل المكلف عبادة كان أو معاملة عقلى أو شرعي ؟ فبناء على هذين التمهدين قال المصنف: الحكم بالصحة في العبادات عقلى ، لأنها (أي الصحة) استتباع الفعل واستلزامه الغاية وفائدة مشروعيته، ثم ذكر الاختلاف في غاية الفعل وقال : ( وهي غاية الفعل ) في العبادات (۱) عند المتكلمين موافقة (الفعل المأموريه) الأمر (أمر الشارع أي كون الفعل المأمور به موافقاً لأمر الشارع هي غاية العبادات عند المتكلمين) وإن وجب القضاء ( في بعض الصور لعدم موافقة أمر الشارع وعدم ترتب الغاية في الحقيقة ( كصلاة من صلى بظن الظهارة ، ثم ظهر أنه كان محدثاً أو غير طاهر الثوب أو غير طاهر البدن ، فيجب عليه القضاء. (۲) والغاية عند الفقهاء كون الفعل مسقطاً لوجوب القضاء، كما في الأداء، تحقيقاً ، فيما كان له قضاء، كصلوات الخمس والصوم، أو تقديراً فيما لا يكون له قضاء، كصلاة العيد وصلاة الجمعة . و بعد ورود ( أمر الشارع ) يعرف ذلك (كونه مسقطاً) بلا توقف على الشرع، كما إذا ورد أمر الشارع بأداء الصلاة ، فإذا أدى الصلاة بشرائطها وأركانها كانت مسقطة للقضاء، فلا يتوقف ذلك على الشرع. وقد ظَن أنها (صحة الفعل) من أحكام الوضع ، أى من الأحكام الوضعية، دون التكليفية، لأن الضحة عبارة عن استتباع الفعل الغاية واستلزامها الفائدة، وإنما يكون الاستتباع بإتمام الفعل، ويأتى الإتمام بفعل جميع الأركان والشروط والشروط و الأركان من جملة الأحكام الوضعية، فتكون الصحة من الأحكام الوضعية. (۳) وقيل الصحة بمعنى موافقة أمر الشارع حكمها عقلى ، و بمعنى الإسقاط اسقاط القضاء حكمها وضعى شرعى، ثم اعترض على القول : بأنها بمعنى الإسقاط وضعى وقال : أقول: الإسقاط (إسقاط القضاء فرع الاتمام، وهو يعرف بالموافقة لأمر الشارع ، وهو (الموافقة) عقلى (لا وضعى). (٤) وقيل ( حكم الصحة) في المعاملات وضعى اتفاقاً، لأن الغاية والغرض من المعاملات كالبيع والشراء والنكاح) ترتب الثمرات (كترتب الملك وتملك البضع) على العقود (عقد البيع والنكاح والإجارة وذلك الترتب موقوف على التوقيف من الشارع البتة، فيكون وضعياً. أقول الخ الغرض منه هو الرد على القول بأن حكم الصحة في المعاملات وضعي، وتفصيله أن في المعاملات أمرين : (۱) جعل العقود أسباباً لترتب الثمرات عليها ، ولا ريب أنه (هذا الجعل ) من وضع الشرع يعرف من الشارع . (۲) وصحة العقود والمعاملات ليست عبارة عن جعلها أسبابا للثمرات الحاصلة منها ، بل صحة العقود عبارة عن الإتيان بها كما جعلها الشارع، ووضع لها طرقاً، وذلك الإتيان الموافق لتفصيل الشارع هو المناط الذى توقف عليه ترتب الثمرات. وهذا معنى قوله : لكن الصخة في العقود هو الإتيان بها كما جعلها ووضعها
Acts of worship according to the theologians (mutakallimin) are [defined as] conformity to the command, even if qada becomes obligatory, like prayer with presumption of purity. According to the jurists (fuqaha), it is [the act] being that which drops the obligation of qada, either actually or presumptively, as in performance. After the command comes, this is known without pause. And it was supposed that [sahih] is from the positive rulings. And it was said: in the sense of conformity, [its ruling] is rational; and in the sense of [dropping] qada, it is positive. I say: dropping qada is a branch of completion, and it is through conformity, and that is rational. And it was said: in transactions, it is unanimously positive, because the arrangement of fruits upon contracts depends entirely on the stipulation [of the Lawgiver]. I say: making contracts causes is undoubtedly with [positive] placement, but sahih is performing them as He made them, and that is the criterion for the entailment of the fruit, and after the law it is known by reason, so reflect. Know first: that the ultimate purpose (ghayah) is the aim of the act and its benefit that occurs and is found at the end of the act. Then that act is of two types: acts of worship, like prayer and fasting, and transactions, like sale and purchase. Then the scholars differed first: what is the ultimate purpose of acts of worship? And second: is the ruling of validity (sahih) of the act of the responsible person, whether worship or transaction, rational or legal? So based on these two premises, the author said: The ruling of validity in acts of worship is rational, because it (i.e., validity) is the act's entailment and requirement of the purpose and benefit of its legislation. Then he mentioned the difference regarding the ultimate purpose of the act and said: 'And it (the ultimate purpose of the act) in acts of worship (1) according to the theologians is conformity of the commanded act to the command (the command of the Lawgiver), i.e., the commanded act being conforming to the command of the Lawgiver is the ultimate purpose of acts of worship according to the theologians, even if qada becomes obligatory in some cases due to lack of conformity to the command of the Lawgiver and lack of actual entailment of the purpose, like the prayer of one who prayed with presumption of purity, then it became apparent that he was impure or his garment was impure or his body was impure, so qada becomes obligatory upon him. (2) The ultimate purpose according to the jurists is the act being that which drops the obligation of qada, as in performance, actually for that which has qada, like the five prayers and fasting, or presumptively for that which has no qada, like the Eid prayer and Friday prayer. And after the command of the Lawgiver comes, that (its being that which drops) is known without reliance on the law, as when the command of the Lawgiver comes to perform prayer, so if he performs the prayer with its conditions and pillars, it becomes that which drops qada, so that does not depend on the law. And it was supposed that it (validity of the act) is from the positive rulings, i.e., from the positive rulings, not the obligating ones, because validity is the act's entailment of the purpose and its requirement of the benefit, and the entailment only occurs by completion of the act, and completion comes by performing all the pillars and conditions, and pillars and conditions are part of the positive rulings, so validity is from the positive rulings. (3) And it was said: validity in the sense of conformity to the command of the Lawgiver, its ruling is rational; and in the sense of dropping qada, its ruling is positive and legal. Then he objected to the statement that it (validity) in the sense of dropping qada is positive, and said: I say: dropping qada is a branch of completion, and it is known through conformity to the command of the Lawgiver, and that (conformity) is rational, not positive. (4) And it was said: the ruling of validity in transactions is unanimously positive, because the ultimate purpose and aim of transactions like sale, purchase, and marriage is the arrangement of fruits like ownership and possession of the woman) upon contracts (contract of sale, marriage, and lease), and that arrangement depends entirely on the stipulation of the Lawgiver, so it is positive. I say: the purpose of that is to refute the statement that the ruling of validity in transactions is positive, and its detail is that in transactions there are two matters: (1) making contracts causes for the arrangement of fruits upon them, and there is no doubt that (this making) is from the positive placement of the law, known from the Lawgiver. (2) And validity of contracts and transactions is not the expression of making them causes for the fruits arising from them; rather, validity of contracts is the expression of performing them as the Lawgiver made them and prescribed ways for them, and that performance conforming to the detail of the Lawgiver is the criterion upon which the arrangement of fruits depends. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'But validity in contracts is performing them as He made and placed them.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Muslim al-Thubut addresses the nature of validity (sahih) in Islamic law, distinguishing between acts of worship (ibadat) and transactions (mu'amalat). The author begins by presenting two definitions of the ultimate purpose (ghayah) of acts. According to the theologians (mutakallimin), the purpose is conformity to the divine command, so if a prayer is performed with presumption of purity but later found invalid, qada is required because the actual conformity was absent. According to the Hanafi jurists (fuqaha), the purpose is that the act drops the obligation of qada, whether actually (for acts with qada like daily prayers) or presumptively (for acts with no qada like Eid prayer). The passage then discusses whether the ruling of validity is rational (aqli) or positive (wad'i, i.e., divinely imposed). For acts of worship, the author of the matn holds that validity is rational because it entails the purpose and benefit, which can be known by reason after the law is revealed. Some argue that validity as conformity is rational, but as dropping qada it is positive. The author rebuts this by showing that dropping qada depends on completion, which is known through conformity, and conformity is rational. For transactions, the common view is that validity is positive because the legal effects (e.g., ownership) depend entirely on the Lawgiver's stipulation. However, the author clarifies that while the causation (making contracts legal causes) is positive, the actual validity (sahih) is performing the contract as the Lawgiver prescribed, and this performance is rational in the sense that it is the criterion for the legal effect. Thus, the passage emphasizes that validity is fundamentally about completion and conformity, and its epistemological status depends on the context.

Key Terms
الصحة (as-sihhah): Validity of an act, meaning its entailment of the intended purpose and benefit; for worship, it is either conformity to command (mutakallimin) or dropping qada (fuqaha); for transactions, it is performing the contract as legally prescribed.
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The ultimate purpose or aim of an act; for worship, it is conformity to command (mutakallimin) or dropping qada (fuqaha); for transactions, it is the legal effect (e.g., ownership).
حكم وضعى (ḥukm waḍʿī): A positive ruling that is divinely imposed and not known by reason alone, such as the causality of contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi jurists (fuqaha) as presented in this passage, what is the ultimate purpose (ghayah) of acts of worship?
A) Conformity to the divine command
B) Dropping the obligation of qada
C) Entailment of worldly benefit
D) Being a cause for reward

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of sahih for worship centers on dropping qada, a practical criterion that differs from the mutakallimin's focus on conformity.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

10 min
Rational vs. Positive Nature of Sahih
Defining sahih and its classification as rational or positive in usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rational and positive aspects of sahih and apply this distinction to acts of worship and transactions.

Core Idea
Sahih has rational elements in worship but positive elements in transactions, reflecting the nature of the act's entailment.

Beyond defining the purpose of acts, scholars debated whether the ruling of validity (sahih) itself is rational (aqli) or positive (wad'i). This lesson unpacks that debate as presented in the passage, focusing on how the epistemological status of sahih differs between acts of worship and transactions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two definitions of ghayah
This lesson builds on the previous one. Recall that for worship, the ghayah according to the Hanafi fuqaha is dropping qada, and for mutakallimin it is conformity to command. For transactions, the ghayah is the legal effect (e.g., ownership).
The ghayah influences whether sahih is rational or positive.
2
Identify the claim about sahih in worship
The author states that the ruling of validity in acts of worship is rational (aqli). Reason: sahih is the act's entailment of its ghayah, and after the law is revealed, reason can determine that entailment by checking if the act was performed completely.
This is the matn's position.
3
Understand the counterclaim and objection
Some argued that sahih in the sense of dropping qada is positive (wad'i) because pillars and conditions are positive rulings. The author objects: dropping qada is a branch of completion, and completion is known through conformity to the command, which is rational.
The author maintains that at its core, validity in worship is rational.
4
Analyze the status in transactions
By common view, the ruling of validity in transactions is unanimously positive because the legal effects (fruits) depend on the Lawgiver's stipulation (tawqif). However, the author refines: making contracts causes is positive, but the actual validity—performing the contract as prescribed—is the criterion for the effect and is known by reason after the law.
Thus, even in transactions, there is a rational aspect to validity.
5
Synthesize the overall position
The author concludes that sahih is fundamentally about performing the act as the Lawgiver intended (completion), and this is rational in the sense that it can be discerned by reason once the legal framework is given. The positive element (wad') pertains to the causation, not the validity itself.
This preserves the rational integrity of evaluating acts.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Muslim al-Thubut addresses the nature of validity (sahih) in Islamic law, distinguishing between acts of worship (ibadat) and transactions (mu'amalat). The author begins by presenting two definitions of the ultimate purpose (ghayah) of acts. According to the theologians (mutakallimin), the purpose is conformity to the divine command, so if a prayer is performed with presumption of purity but later found invalid, qada is required because the actual conformity was absent. According to the Hanafi jurists (fuqaha), the purpose is that the act drops the obligation of qada, whether actually (for acts with qada like daily prayers) or presumptively (for acts with no qada like Eid prayer). The passage then discusses whether the ruling of validity is rational (aqli) or positive (wad'i, i.e., divinely imposed). For acts of worship, the author of the matn holds that validity is rational because it entails the purpose and benefit, which can be known by reason after the law is revealed. Some argue that validity as conformity is rational, but as dropping qada it is positive. The author rebuts this by showing that dropping qada depends on completion, which is known through conformity, and conformity is rational. For transactions, the common view is that validity is positive because the legal effects (e.g., ownership) depend entirely on the Lawgiver's stipulation. However, the author clarifies that while the causation (making contracts legal causes) is positive, the actual validity (sahih) is performing the contract as the Lawgiver prescribed, and this performance is rational in the sense that it is the criterion for the legal effect. Thus, the passage emphasizes that validity is fundamentally about completion and conformity, and its epistemological status depends on the context.

Key Terms
الصحة (as-sihhah): Validity of an act, meaning its entailment of the intended purpose and benefit; for worship, it is either conformity to command (mutakallimin) or dropping qada (fuqaha); for transactions, it is performing the contract as legally prescribed.
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The ultimate purpose or aim of an act; for worship, it is conformity to command (mutakallimin) or dropping qada (fuqaha); for transactions, it is the legal effect (e.g., ownership).
حكم وضعى (ḥukm waḍʿī): A positive ruling that is divinely imposed and not known by reason alone, such as the causality of contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's objection in the passage, why is the validity of an act of worship in the sense of dropping qada ultimately rational, not positive?
A) Because dropping qada is a branch of completion, and completion is known through conformity, which is rational
B) Because the Lawgiver's command is known by reason
C) Because all positive rulings are actually rational
D) Because pillars and conditions are rational

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, sahih has a rational core even in transactions, where the common view underestimates the role of reason.

The Dual Nature of Validity: Rational and Positive Aspects
Analyzing whether validity is wad‘i or ‘aqli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the wad‘i (positive) and ‘aqli (rational) aspects of validity (sihha) and apply this distinction to an act of worship or a transaction.

Core Idea
Validity is part rational (conformity to command) and part positive (entailment of effects) – the nature depends on the level of abstraction.

In Hanafi usul, the concept of validity (sihha) is not a single kind of ruling. The passage you are about to study unravels its layers: as conformity to the Lawgiver's command, validity is a rational judgment; as a cause for removing the need to make up a missed worship or for transferring ownership, it is a positive (wad‘i) ruling set by the Lawgiver. This distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing both acts of worship and transactions, and it reveals a sophisticated understanding of how reason and revelation interact in legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of sihha
From the passage, extract the two distinct senses: (a) sihha as conformity to the Lawgiver's command, and (b) sihha as entailing a legal effect (e.g., removal of qada' or transfer of ownership).
The passage uses 'موافقة أمر الشارع' for conformity and 'إسقاط القضاء' for removal of the obligation to make up.
2
Determine which aspect is rational and which is positive
Note that conformity to a command is a rational judgment – reason tells you that if you did what was commanded, you have complied. The entailment of a legal effect, however, depends on the Lawgiver's designation, making it a positive ruling.
The passage explicitly says: 'الصحة بمعنى موافقة أمر الشارع حكمها عقلى ، و بمعنى الإسقاط حكمها وضعى شرعى'.
3
Apply the universal-particular framework
Understand that the validity of a particular instance (e.g., Zayd's sale) is assessed by matching it to the universal (the general contract of sale as defined by the Lawgiver). That matching is a rational act. In contrast, the validity of the universal category itself (sale as a legal institution) depends on the Lawgiver's making it a cause for ownership – that is a wad‘i ruling.
The passage states: 'صحة الجزئيات بالمطابقة للكليات، وصحة الكليات بالجعل من الشارع'.
4
Recognize the mistake of treating sihha as purely wad‘i
Some scholars think that because pillars and conditions are wad‘i, sihha must also be purely wad‘i. The passage refutes this by showing that the entailment (istitba‘) is a rational consequence, not a legal one.
The author says: 'الاستتباع عقلى لا وضعى'.

And after the occurrence of (the command of the Lawgiver), that (its being a remover of obligation) is known without needing to rely on the law, just as when the command of the Lawgiver comes to perform the prayer, so if he performs the prayer with its conditions and essential parts, it becomes remover of [the need for] making up, so that does not depend on the law. And it was thought that it (the validity of the act) is from the rulings of wad‘, i.e. from the positive rulings, not the prescriptive rulings, because validity is the act's entailing the goal and requiring the benefit, and the entailing occurs only by completing the act, and completion comes by performing all the essential parts and conditions, and the conditions and essential parts are among the positive rulings, so validity is from the positive rulings. (3) And it was said: validity in the sense of conformity to the command of the Lawgiver, its ruling is rational, and in the sense of removal (of the need to make up) its ruling is positive and legal. Then he objected to the statement that it is in the sense of removal, saying: I say: removal (removal of the need to make up) is a branch of completion, and completion is known by conformity to the command of the Lawgiver, and that (conformity) is rational, not positive. (4) And it was said: (the ruling of validity) in transactions is positive by agreement, because the aim and purpose of transactions like sale, purchase, and marriage is the attachment of the fruits (like ownership and the ownership of the vagina) to the contracts (the contract of sale, marriage, and lease), and that attachment depends absolutely on the designation from the Lawgiver, so it is positive. I say etc. Its purpose is to refute the claim that the ruling of validity in transactions is positive, and its detail is that in transactions there are two matters: (1) making contracts causes for the attachment of fruits to them, and there is no doubt that (this making) is from the law's designation, known from the Lawgiver. (2) And the validity of contracts and transactions is not the making of them causes for the fruits resulting from them; rather, validity of contracts is the performance of them as the Lawgiver made them and laid down paths for them, and that performance conforming to the specification of the Lawgiver is the criterion on which depends the attachment of fruits. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'but validity in contracts is the performance of them as the Lawgiver made and placed them,' and that is the criterion for entailing and requiring the fruits, and it (the performance as the Lawgiver made it) occurs after the law and is known by reason. His statement: 'so reflect on it' is an indication that here there are particulars and universals: validity of particulars is by conformity to universals, and validity of universals is by the making from the Lawgiver, and similarly in acts of worship, so validity of particulars is by conformity to universals, and validity of universals is by conformity to the command (the command of the Lawgiver). Meaning that in transactions and contracts there are universals like the contract of sale and marriage, and particulars like the sale of Zayd and the marriage of ‘Amr. So the validity of particular contracts is by correspondence with the universals, and the validity of universals is by conformity to the command of the Lawgiver. So the ruling of validity of particulars is rational, and the ruling of validity of universals is positive.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the concept of validity (sihha) in Islamic jurisprudence, specifically whether it is a positive (wad‘i) or rational (‘aqli) ruling. The discussion is part of a commentary on the matn 'Musallam al-Thubut'. The author begins by presenting the view that validity, in the sense of being a cause for the removal of the obligation to make up (isqat al-qada'), is known rationally after the command of the Lawgiver, without needing further legal stipulation. For example, if the Lawgiver commands a prayer, and one performs it with all its conditions and pillars, then that performance logically entails the removal of the need to make it up. This rational entailment does not require a separate legal ruling. However, another view holds that validity is a positive (wad‘i) ruling because it consists of completing the act through its pillars and conditions, which are themselves wad‘i rulings. The author then distinguishes two meanings of 'sihha': (a) conformity to the Lawgiver's command (muwafaqat amr al-shari‘), which is a rational judgment, and (b) the removal of the need to make up (isqat al-qada'), which is a positive legal ruling. An objection is raised that even isqat al-qada' is a branch of completion, and completion is known by conformity, which is rational, so isqat al-qada' is ultimately rational. The discussion then shifts to transactions (mu‘amalat). Some claim that validity in transactions is purely wad‘i because the fruits (e.g., ownership) only arise through the Lawgiver's designation. The author refines this by distinguishing two aspects: (1) the act of making contracts causes for the fruits—this is purely a wad‘i act of the Lawgiver; (2) the validity of a particular contract is performing it in the manner prescribed by the Lawgiver, and this conformity is rational. Thus, validity in transactions has both a wad‘i and a rational dimension. The author concludes by applying a universal-particular framework: the validity of particular contracts (e.g., Zayd's sale) is rational because it is judged by matching the universal (the general contract of sale as defined by the Lawgiver); the validity of the universal itself (the general category of sale) is wad‘i because it depends on the Lawgiver's making it a cause. This same analysis applies to acts of worship. Therefore, the ruling of validity is not exclusively wad‘i or ‘aqli; it depends on the level of abstraction.

Key Terms
الصحة (al-sihha): Validity of an act, meaning the act's entailment of its intended consequence (e.g., removal of qada' or transfer of ownership) through conformity to the Lawgiver's command.
الحكم الوضعي (al-hukm al-wad‘i): A ruling that establishes a thing as a cause, condition, or preventer for another legal effect, known by revelation.
الحكم العقلي (al-hukm al-‘aqli): A ruling that reason independently arrives at without needing revelation, such as the logical entailment of a cause and effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of validity of a particular contract like 'the sale of Zayd'?
A) It is a purely wad‘i ruling.
B) It is a purely ‘aqli ruling.
C) It is both wad‘i and ‘aqli equally.
D) It has no ruling until the Lawgiver specifies.

Key Takeaway
Validity is not a single type of ruling; it has a rational facet (conformity to command) and a wad‘i facet (entailment of legal effects), and both are necessary for a complete understanding of sihha.

Obligating the Impossible: Hanafi Usul on Capacity
Defining المحكوم فيه and ruling on imposing impossible acts on the mukallaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on obligating impossible acts and distinguish between types of impossibility using the proof from Quran 2:286.

Core Idea
The Lawgiver cannot obligate the impossible; obligation is tied to capacity.

This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work defines the subject of legal ruling (al-mahkum fihi) as the act of the mukallaf. It immediately addresses a critical issue: can the Lawgiver impose an obligation that is impossible for the mukallaf to perform? The Hanafi school gives a decisive answer based on Quranic text and consensus, distinguishing between types of impossibility and engaging with opposing views from the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of impossibility
From the passage, list the three categories: impossibility by essence (e.g., combining opposites), impossibility by incapacity (e.g., creating a substance), and impossibility by custom (e.g., carrying a mountain).
Also referred to as mumtani' li-dhatihi, mumtani' li-ghayrihi, and mumtani' 'ādatan.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each type
For essence and incapacity: absolutely impermissible to obligate. For custom: rationally possible but legally impermissible.
Legal impermissibility here means the obligation is not valid (ghayr sahīh).
3
Locate the primary proof
The Quranic verse 2:286 ('Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity') is cited as the main dalil for the prohibition.
The Hanafis treat this as general and binding (amm qat'i).
4
Contrast the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris theoretically allow obligating the impossible, though they differ on its actual occurrence. The Hanafis reply that obligation presupposes possibility of compliance.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis reject any fiqh ruling based on impossible obligations; Ash'aris leave the door open theoretically.
5
Distinguish real vs. formal obligation
Real obligation (taklif haqiqi) is genuine demand; formal obligation (suri) is mere utterance without intent, not considered valid taklif.
The passage ends by clarifying that 'impossible' here refers to acts that cannot emanate from the mukallaf.

The third chapter: On the subject of the ruling (al-mahkum fihi), which is the act of the mukallaf. The subject of the ruling: it is the act of the mukallaf concerning which the Lawgiver has ruled — i.e., concerning its status — with obligation, prohibition, recommendation, dislike, or permissibility. Or we say: it is the act to which the speech of Allah Most High relates, as a demand or selection. (1) Issue: On the impermissibility of imposing an obligation involving something impossible due to its essence or due to something else. Imposing an obligation involving the impossible is absolutely impermissible, such as combining opposites, or due to the mukallaf, such as creating a substance with created power. The Ash'aris permitted it and disagreed about its occurrence. As for what is impossible by custom, such as carrying a mountain, it is rationally permissible, contrary to the Mu'tazila, and it is not legally permissible, because of the verse: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286). Consensus is established on the validity of imposing obligation with something that Allah knows will not occur, because if it were valid it would be demanded, and demand is dependent on imagining its occurrence; as it is demanded, otherwise it would not be demanded but rather something else. This is necessary. Imagining the occurrence of the impossible insofar as it is impossible in the external world is necessarily invalid. This concerns real obligation and real demand. As for formal obligation — that the Lawgiver pronounces the command form and says 'bring about the impossible' or 'bring together two opposites' — it is nothing but like your saying 'two opposites are combined'. It is said to be impossible only due to another perception; if that perception were correct, it would be correct, so reflect. And know that what is meant by 'impossible' here is an act whose occurrence from the mukallaf is impossible, either due to its essence, or...
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter of a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work, defining 'المحكوم فيه' (the subject of the ruling) as the act of the legally responsible person (mukallaf) that is directly addressed by the Lawgiver's command or option. Then the author immediately discusses a foundational issue: the permissibility of imposing obligations that are impossible. He categorizes impossibility into three types: (1) intrinsically impossible (e.g., combining opposites), (2) impossible due to the subject's incapacity (e.g., creating a substance with finite power), and (3) conventionally impossible (e.g., carrying a mountain). The Hanafi position is that the first two types cannot be legally obligated at all; the third type is rationally possible but not legally permissible because Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity (Quran 2:286). The Ash'aris argue that even intrinsically impossible acts could be commanded in theory, though they disagree about actual occurrence. The Mu'tazila hold that conventionally impossible acts are also rationally impossible. The author distinguishes between real obligation (actual demand intended by the Lawgiver) and formal obligation (mere utterance of command words without real intent), noting that the latter is not a genuine demand. The passage ends by clarifying that 'impossible' here refers to acts that cannot emanate from the mukallaf either due to their own nature or due to external factors.

Key Terms
المحكوم فيه (al-mahkum fihi): The act of the mukallaf that is the subject of a Shari'a ruling (obligation, prohibition, etc.).
الممتنع (al-mumtani'): An act that is impossible to occur from the mukallaf, either inherently or due to external constraints.
التكليف الحقيقي (al-taklif al-haqiqi): Real obligation intended by the Lawgiver, as opposed to a mere verbal command without genuine demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on obligating conventionally impossible acts (like carrying a mountain)?
A) Rationally and legally impermissible
B) Rationally permissible but legally impermissible
C) Legally permissible but rationally impossible
D) Permissible absolutely

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school firmly prohibits obligating any act that the mukallaf cannot perform, based on Quran 2:286 and consensus.

Taklif bi al-Mumtani' (Imposing Impossible Actions) in Hanafi Usul
Definition of mahkum fihi and analysis of impossibility in taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of impossible actions (mumtani') and apply the Hanafi ruling on taklif for each type, citing the Quranic and rational proofs.

Core Idea
Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity: impositions that are inherently or habitually impossible are invalid in Shari'.

This passage opens the third chapter of a Hanafi usul work, defining the subject of the ruling (al-mahkum fihi) and then tackling a key debate: can Allah command an impossible act? The Hanafi school, grounded in the Quranic verse 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها' (2:286), distinguishes four types of impossibility. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying the principle that taklif must be within the mukallaf's capacity, a cornerstone of Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Define al-mahkum fihi
Identify the subject of the legal rule (the action of the mukallaf) and its five possible rulings according to the Lawgiver.
This sets the stage for the specific issue of impossible actions.
2
Classify the four types of impossibility
From the passage, list the four types of mumtani': inherently impossible, impossible due to lack of capacity, habitually impossible, and impossible due to divine knowledge of non-occurrence.
Each type has a different ruling on taklif in the Hanafi school.
3
State the Hanafi ruling for each type
For types 1 and 2, taklif is absolutely impermissible; for type 3, rationally possible but prohibited by Shari'; for type 4, valid by consensus.
Note the opposing views of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.
4
Analyze the Quranic proof
Apply the verse 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها' as evidence: impossible actions are beyond capacity, so taklif with them is invalid.
Consider the thubut (qat'i) and dalala (qat'i) of this verse.
5
Evaluate the rational proof
Follow the Maturidi/verifying Ash'ari argument: demand requires conceiving the demanded act as possible; for the impossible, this conception is self-contradictory, proving the invalidity of taklif.
This rational proof complements the naqli proof.

-٤ - و ممتنع لأجل علمه تعالى بأنه لا يقع،
فذكر المصنف تحت عنوان ( المسألة ) حكم هذه الأنواع الأربعة :
١- لا يجوز التكليف بالفعل الممتنع مطلقا (سواء كان امتناعه لذاته كالجمع بين الضدين (العدم والوجود) أو كان امتناعه من أجل عدم قدرة المكلف عليه، كخلق الجواهر (والأجسام) (فإنه ممتنع) من القدرة الحادثة (كقدرة الإنس و الجن والملك) وجوز الأشعرية التكليف ( بالنوعين الأولين من الممتنع) واختلفوا (الأشاعرة) في وقوعه ( وقوع التكليف بالممتنع شرعاً ، ولكن لم يأتوا بالمثال في صورة الوقوع) -۲- واما النوع الثالث الممتنع عادة ، كحمل الجبال فيجوز عندنا التكليف به عقلاً ، خلافاً للمعتزلة (فإنّهم لا يقولون بجواز التكليف بالممتنع العادى عقلاً ولا يجوز عندنا التكليف بالممتنع العادى شرعاً، ودليل عدم جواز التكليف بالأنواع الثلاثة قولة تعالى: (لا يكلف نفساً إلا وسعها ) - واما النوع الرابع ( الممتنع لأجل علمه تعالى بعدم وقوعه) فالإجماع منعقد على صحة التكليف بما علم الله تعالى أنه لا يقع - كتكليف أبي جهل وأمثاله كمن ماتوا على الكفر ، وقال تعالى في حقهم : (لقد حق القول على اكثرهم فهم لا يؤمنون) وكان هؤلاء مكلفين بالإيمان بالتوحيد والرسالة والقيامة.
دليل الماتريدية و محققى الأشاعرة
(والدليل ( لنا (أنه ( لو صح التكليف بالممتنع بنوعيه ، لكان ذلك الممتنع مطلوباً من المكلف) والطلب (اولاً)، والإتيان به ثانياً) موقوف على تصوّر وقوعه كما طلب
Chapter Three: On the Subject of the Ruling (al-Mahkum Fihi), which is the action of the obligated person. Al-Mahkum fihi: it is the action of the obligated person concerning which the Lawgiver has ruled, i.e., regarding its status, as obligatory, prohibited, recommended, disliked, or permissible. Or we may say: it is the action to which Allah's address pertains as either a demand or an option.
(1) Issue on the impermissibility of imposing an obligation for something impossible in itself or due to another factor. Imposing an obligation for the impossible is absolutely impermissible, such as combining two opposites, or [an action] beyond the capacity of the obligated person, like creating a substance from created power. The Ash'aris permitted it, and they disagreed about its occurrence. As for that which is habitually impossible, like carrying a mountain, it is rationally permissible—contrary to the Mu'tazila—but not permitted by sacred law, due to His statement: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' Consensus is established on the validity of imposing an obligation for something that Allah knows will not occur. For us, if it were valid, it would be demanded, and the demand is dependent on conceiving its occurrence as demanded; otherwise, he would not demand that, but something else. This is self-evident. Conception of the impossible occurring, insofar as it is impossible in external reality, is necessarily false. This pertains to real obligation and genuine demand.
As for formal [obligation], such that the Lawgiver utters the imperative form and says 'Bring about the impossible' or 'Produce a conjunction of opposites,' that is nothing but like saying 'The conjunction of opposites is actual.'
It is said to be impossible due to another consideration; if that consideration were complete, it would be complete, so ponder.
Know that what is meant by 'impossible' here is an action whose occurrence from the obligated person is unrealizable either in itself, or due to something else, or due to His knowledge that it will not issue from the obligated person. From this you know that the impossible is of four types:
1. Impossible in itself, like combining two opposites.
2. Impossible relative to the capacity of the obligated person, like creating bodies, which he expressed as creating substances.
3. Habitually impossible, like carrying a mountain.
4. Impossible due to His knowledge that it will not occur.
So the author, under the heading 'the issue', mentioned the ruling on these four types:
1. Imposing an obligation for the impossible action is absolutely impermissible, whether its impossibility is in itself (like combining two opposites—non-existence and existence) or its impossibility arises from the lack of capacity of the obligated person (like creating substances and bodies, for it is impossible from created power, such as the power of humans, jinn, and angels). The Ash'aris permitted obligation for the first two types of the impossible, and they (the Ash'aris) disagreed about its occurrence (i.e., the occurrence of obligation for the impossible in sacred law, but they did not provide an example of its occurrence). 2. As for the third type, the habitually impossible, like carrying mountains, it is rationally permissible in our view to impose such an obligation, contrary to the Mu'tazila (for they do not consider it rationally permissible to impose an obligation for the habitually impossible). It is not permitted in our view to impose an obligation for the habitually impossible by sacred law. The evidence for the impermissibility of imposing an obligation for the three types is the statement of the Exalted: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' 3. As for the fourth type (the impossible due to His knowledge that it will not occur), consensus is established on the validity of imposing an obligation for what Allah knows will not occur—like the obligation on Abu Jahl and his like among those who died in disbelief. Allah said concerning them: 'The word has been justified against most of them, so they do not believe.' These people were obligated to believe in monotheism, prophethood, and the resurrection.
The proof of the Maturidis and the verifying Ash'aris: (The proof for us is that) if the obligation for the impossible were valid in its two types, then that impossible would be demanded from the obligated person. The demand (first) and the performance (second) are dependent on conceiving its occurrence as it is demanded.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter of the work, defining al-mahkum fihi (the subject of the ruling) as the action of the mukallaf (legally responsible person) that has been directly ruled upon by the Lawgiver—whether as obligatory, prohibited, recommended, disliked, or permissible. It then introduces a major usul issue: whether it is permissible for Allah to command an act that is impossible. The author states the Hanafi position that taklif (imposing an obligation) with a mumtani' (impossible act) is absolutely impermissible. He then categorizes impossibility into four types: (1) inherently impossible (e.g., combining existence and non-existence), (2) impossible relative to human capacity (e.g., creating a substance), (3) habitually impossible (e.g., carrying a mountain), and (4) impossible due to Allah's knowledge that it will not occur (e.g., Abu Jahl's belief). The ruling differs: types 1 and 2 are absolutely impermissible for taklif; type 3 is rationally possible but not permitted by Shari' (based on Qur'an 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها'); type 4 is valid by consensus, as seen in the obligation of disbelievers to believe even though Allah knew they would not. The passage then provides a rational proof (attributed to the Maturidis and verifying Ash'aris): if taklif with the impossible were valid, the act would be demanded; but demand requires conceiving the act as possible, which for the impossible is self-contradictory. This aligns with the Hanafi usul position that taklif must be with something conceivable. Note that Ash'aris allowed taklif with types 1 and 2 rationally but differed on its occurrence in Shari', while Mu'tazila denied even rational possibility for type 3.

Key Terms
المحكوم فيه (al-mahkum fihi): The action of the mukallaf concerning which the Lawgiver has issued a ruling (obligatory, prohibited, etc.).
التكليف (al-taklif): Imposing an obligation or demand upon a mukallaf; the act of commanding or prohibiting.
الممتنع (al-mumtani'): That which is impossible; an action that cannot occur from the mukallaf either inherently, due to capacity, habit, or divine knowledge.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The category of legal obligation; one of the five rulings (ahkam) that can apply to an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, which type of impossible action is valid for taklif (legal obligation) by consensus?
A) Inherently impossible (like combining opposites)
B) Impossible relative to human capacity (like creating a substance)
C) Habitually impossible (like carrying a mountain)
D) Impossible due to Allah's knowledge that it will not occur (like Abu Jahl's belief)

Key Takeaway
Taklif with the impossible is invalid except when Allah knows it will not occur; this distinction is built on the Quranic principle of capacity and rational necessity.

Classification of the Impossible and Ruling on Obliging the Impossible
Classifies four types of impossible and rules on taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of impossible acts and apply the Hanafi principle that only the fourth type (impossible due to Allah's knowledge) can be legitimately obligated.

Core Idea
An impossible act cannot be genuinely obligated—only formal commands and those known to Allah are exceptions.

This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut classifies the impossible into four categories and explains which can be obligated. The Hanafi school holds that the first three cannot be genuinely demanded, while the fourth—impossible only because Allah knows it will not happen—is valid by consensus. This lesson will help you understand the rational and textual proofs for this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of impossible
List the four types: impossible in itself, by capacity, customarily, and by divine knowledge.
These are foundational categories in Hanafi usul.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each type
For types 1-3: impermissible; type 4: permissible by consensus.
3
Analyze the proof for impermissibility
Examine the Quranic verse (2:286) and the rational argument against obligating the impossible.
The proof is both naqli and aqli.
4
Compare with Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions
Note that Ash'aris permit types 1-2 rationally, Mu'tazila reject type 3 rationally, but Hanafis reject types 1-3 both rationally and legally.
5
Distinguish genuine vs. formal obligation
Understand that formal commands (without intent) do not entail impossibility.
This distinction resolves objections about divine speech without intent.

From this it is known that the impossible is of four types: 1) Impossible in itself, like combining two opposites. 2) Impossible relative to the capacity of the obligated person, like creating bodies, which is expressed as creating atomic substances. 3) Customarily impossible, like carrying a mountain. 4) Impossible due to Allah's knowledge that it will not occur. Then the author under the heading (The Issue) mentioned the ruling of these four types: 1) Obligation of an impossible act is not permissible absolutely (whether its impossibility is in itself like combining two opposites (non-existence and existence) or its impossibility is due to the lack of capacity of the obligated person, like creating atomic substances (and bodies) (for it is impossible) for created power (like the power of humans, jinn, and angels)). The Ash'aris permitted obligation of the first two types of impossible, and they (the Ash'aris) differed regarding its occurrence (the occurrence of the obligation of the impossible legally, but they did not provide an example in the case of occurrence). 2) As for the third type, customarily impossible like carrying mountains, we consider its obligation rationally permissible, contrary to the Mu'tazila (for they do not say that obligation of the customarily impossible is rationally permissible) and we do not consider its obligation legally permissible. The proof for the impermissibility of obligation of the three types is His statement: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' 3) As for the fourth type (impossible due to His knowledge that it will not occur), there is consensus on the validity of obligation of what Allah knows will not occur – like the obligation on Abu Jahl and his likes who died upon disbelief. Allah said regarding them: 'The word has been fulfilled against most of them, so they do not believe.' And these people were obligated to believe in tawheed, prophethood, and resurrection. The proof of the Maturidis and verified Ash'aris: (The proof for us is that (if the obligation of the impossible were valid for its two types, then that impossible would be demanded from the obligated person) and the demand (firstly) and its performance (secondly) depend on conceiving its occurrence as demanded (that is, in the state of impossibility and prevention of occurrence). Otherwise (even if it was not demanded in its original state), that impossible would not be demanded, but rather something else would be demanded. So the demand was for something, and what came about was something else. And this (the existence of something other than what is demanded in this case) is necessary and self-evident, needing no proof. And conceiving the possibility of occurrence of the impossible, insofar as it is impossible in the external world, is false by necessity and self-evidence. And this, i.e., the falsehood of the occurrence of the impossible (insofar as it is impossible) in the external world, applies only to actual obligation and true demand. As for formal obligation and apparent demand, such that the commanding obligator utters the imperative form (without intending the demand) and says: 'Bring about the impossible, or you are combining opposites,' then that – i.e., this command and formal obligation – is nothing but like your saying: 'The combination of opposites exists,' so it becomes a command and obligation to bring about this sentence in form, in speech and utterance, only, without reality and actual occurrence, such that the impossible would be necessitated. The reason for preventing obligation of the impossible in reality is that the speech that conveys no benefit – is it a deficiency such that it is impossible for Him, as held by the majority, or not? This is the statement of the author in his commentary. But in my view (according to Shaykh al-Martonki [؟]), the reason is what I mentioned (which is the lack of capacity of the obligated person, the impossibility of the act emanating from him, the futility, and that speaking without intent is a deficiency, and impossible for Him). So if that reason and proof are established, the impossibility is established; otherwise no. And his statement: 'So reflect on it' is an indication of the answer to the objection of futility and deficiency by speaking without intent, because the command and obligation may be for bringing about the act, and may be for showing the inability of the commanded person, as in His statement: 'Say: Be stones or iron or a creation greater than what is in your hearts...'
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut discusses the classification of the impossible (al-mumtani‘) and the ruling on obligating it (taklif bi al-muhal). The author first divides the impossible into four types: (1) impossible in itself (e.g., combining existence and non-existence), (2) impossible relative to the capacity of the creature (e.g., creating matter), (3) customarily impossible (e.g., carrying a mountain), and (4) impossible due to Allah’s knowledge that it will not occur (e.g., faith from Abu Jahl). The Hanafi position is that obligating the first three types is impermissible: the first two are rationally and legally impossible, while the third is rationally possible but legally impermissible. The fourth type is valid by consensus, as shown by the obligation of faith upon those whom Allah knew would not believe. The proof for the first three is the verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (Quran 2:286). The author then presents the rational proof of the Maturidis and verified Ash‘aris: if obligating the impossible were valid, the impossible act would be demanded, but its occurrence is impossible, leading to a demand for something impossible and the occurrence of something else—this is absurd, as it is self-evident that the impossible cannot occur. However, this applies only to genuine obligation; formal obligation, where the command is uttered without intent, is mere speech and does not entail impossibility. Finally, a distinction is made: the command may be for actual performance or to demonstrate the inability of the commanded person, as in the verse 'Say: Be stones or iron...'.

Key Terms
الممتنع (al-mumtani‘): ما لا يمكن وقوعه في الخارج لذاته أو لقدرة المكلف أو للعادة أو لعلم الله
التكليف (al-taklif): الأمر بما فيه كلفة على المكلف، ويطلق على الخطاب الإلهي المتعلق بفعل العبد
الممتنع لذاته (al-mumtani‘ li dhatih): ما يستحيل وجوده لذاته كالجمع بين الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) ممتنع لذاته (impossible in itself)
B) ممتنع عادي (customarily impossible)
C) ممتنع لأجل علمه تعالى (impossible due to Allah's knowledge)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Only impossible acts due to divine knowledge can be legitimately obligated; all other impossibilities render obligation invalid.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
