Hanafi Refutation of Denying Taklif of Kafir with Furu'
Hanafi defense of kafir being obligated with furu' via refutation of three objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a kafir is obligated with furu' and refute three opposing arguments using qiyas, the concept of intrinsic possibility, and the principle that Islam wipes out past obligations.

Core Idea
Obligation does not require valid performance; a kafir is still obligated with furu'.

This passage dives into a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: Is a disbeliever (kafir) obligated with the practical branches of Sharia (furu')? Some scholars argue that since a kafir's acts are invalid without faith, there is no obligation. The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa and his companions, firmly holds that the kafir is mukallaf with furu' and will be punished for neglecting them. Here, the author systematically refutes three evidence of the deniers, using precise usul tools. You will learn to apply analogical reasoning (qiyas), distinguish intrinsic from conditional possibility, and understand how Islam's wiping effect resolves the qada' objection.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the two positions: al-nafi (denier) and al-muthbit (affirmer). Note the thamarah al-khilaf: whether a new Muslim must make up missed acts.
The Hanafi position is that the kafir is obligated but his acts are not valid without faith.
2
Analyze the first argument and its refutation
The denier argues that if obligated, the act would be valid; but validity requires faith. Hanafi refutes by qiyas to junub: a junub is obligated to pray but prayer is invalid without ghusl. So condition does not negate obligation.
The refutation uses naqd (refutation by counterexample) and then provides a positive solution (hal).
3
Analyze the second argument and the concept of intrinsic possibility
The denier argues that compliance (imtithal) must be possible; but a kafir can neither comply during disbelief (invalid intention) nor after Islam (no demand). Hanafi responds by distinguishing conditional impossibility from intrinsic possibility: the kafir can intrinsically comply, though conditioned on faith. This is analogous to a writer's finger movement being necessarily conditioned on writing but intrinsically possible otherwise.
Key term: al-imkan al-dhati – intrinsic possibility independent of external conditions.
4
Analyze the third argument and its refutation
The denier argues that if obligated, qada' would be necessary after Islam, but it is not required by consensus. Hanafi denies the entailment, citing the hadith that 'Islam wipes out what came before.' Thus, qada' is not required; Islam itself serves as a form of qada' or it is a new command.
The passage ends mid-refutation; the complete Hanafi position is that Islam cancels past obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
Yov إزالة الرهبوت عن مشكلات مسلم الثبوت فقط، أي فيؤاخذون على ترك الاعتقاد فقط، وليست هذه المسألة مسألة التكليف بالاعتقاد و الأداء أو الاعتقاد فقط ) محفوظة و منقولة ( عن المتقدمين ( أبي حنيفة وأصحابه ، وإنّما استنبطها البخار يون من قول محمد في المبسوط (في مسألة ) وهي من نذر صوم شهر ، ثم ارتد لم يلزمه ذلك الصوم بعد ما آمن ، فعلم أن الكفر يبطل وجوب أداء العبادات، وفي فقهنا الحنفي مثل هذه المسألة المستنبطة من أقوال الأئمة كثيرة جداً.
أدلة النافى تكليف الكافر بالفروع
أولاً لوصح لصحت منه، لموافقة الأمر، واللازم باطل اتفاقاً، قلنا : منقوض بالجنب، والحل أنه بالشرط كالمحدث
وثانياً: لأمكن الامتثال وفي الكفر لا يمكن، وبعده لا طلب قلنا: ممكن حين الكفر، وإن لم يمكن بشرط الكفر، والضرورة الشرطية لاتنافي الإمكان الذاتي وينقض بالإيمان
وثالثاً: لوجب القضاء ولا يجب اتفاقاً قلنا : الملازمة ممنوعة، فإن الإسلام يجب ما قبله، فهو كأنه قضاء عن الكل، أو أنه بأمر جديد
واستدل النافون لتكليف الكافر بالفروع بثلاثة أوجه: ولهذا قال المصنف:
-
-۱ (والدليل) للتافي :أولاً : أن ( تكليف الكافر بالفروع) لو صح لصحت تلك الفروع (أي يلزم صحة أدائها ) منه، لموافقة الأمر (أمر الشارع المكلف ) إذ الصحة هي موافقة أمر الشارع ، واللازم (صحة أداء تلك الفروع من الكافر) باطل اتفاقاً النافيين والمثبتين ؛ إذ صحتها موقوفة على الإيمان
قلنا في ردّ دليل النافي ( إنه منقوض بالجنب فإنه مكلف بالصلاة حين الجنابة ولا تصح أداء صلوته مع الجنابة (كما لا يصح أداء صلاة الكافر مع الكفر)، فعدم صحة الأداء لا ينافي التكليف ، والحلّ من جانب النافي أنها أي صحة الفروع من الكافر إنما تكون بالشرط وهو الإيمان ، كالمحدث فإنه مكلف بالصلاة بشرط الطهارة، كما يكون
 TOA إزالة الرهبوت عن مشكلات مسلم الثبوت الجنب مكلفاً بالصلاة بشرط الظهارة.
٢ - والدليل له (للنا في ( ثانياً: أنه لو صح تكليف الكافر بالفروع لأمكن الامتثال بتلك الفروع؛ لأن الإمكان شرط التكليف فلا ينفك عنه، والامتثال من الكافر ليس بممكن؛ لأن إمكان الامتثال لا يخلو عن الحالين إما أن يكون حال الكفر ، أو يكون حال الإسلام وترك الكفر، وفي حال الكفر لا يمكن الامتثال لأنه لابد في الامتثال من النية، ونية الكافر غير معتبرة، وكذلك لا يمكن الامتثال بعد ترك الكفر واختيار الإسلام أيضاً، لأن الكافر إذا أسلم لايُطلب منه قضاء ما مضى من الواجبات ؛ لأن الإسلام يهدم ماكان قبله من الذنوب و الواجبات البدنية والمالية. فسقط عنه الأمر والامتثال
فإنّ الامتثال فرع الأمر. قلنا في الجواب عن هذا الدليل : إنا لا نسلّم أن الامتثال حال الكفر غير ممكن ، بل هو ممكن حين الكفر أيضاً لأن ثبوت الكفر له ليس بضرورى ، وإن لم يمكن بشرط وجود الكفر، وضرورة عدم الامتثال بشرط الكفر لاتنا في الإمكان الذاتي ، أي كون الامتثال ممكناً بالذات ، وهذا شرح قوله: "والضرورة الشرطية لا تنافي الإمكان الذاتي نظيره : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بضرورة مادام كاتباً، فثبوت تحرّك الأصابع للكاتب ضرورى بشرط وصف الكتابة، وأما في حد ذاته فسلب تحرك الأصابع عنه ممكن إذالم يكن فيه وصف الكتابة. و ينتقض دليل النافي بالتكليف بالإيمان ، فإنّ الامتثال بالأمر بالإيمان في حال الكفر غير ممكن، و بعد الإيمان وزوال الكفر طلب الايمان والأمر به طلب تحصيل الحاصل. -٣ والدليل له ثالثاً : أنه لوصح تكليف الكافر بالفروع لكانت تلك الفروع مطلوبة من الكافر. وإذا كانت مطلوبة لوجب القضاء عليه، أي قضاء الفروع على الكافر بعد الإسلام، ولا يجب القضاء على الكافر بعد اسلامه اتفاقاً ؛ لأن الإسلام يهدم ما كان قبله.
قلنا في الجواب عن هذا الدليل: الملازمة (لزوم القضاء على الكافر لأجل كونه مكلفاً بالفروع ممنوع فإنّ الإسلام يحب ويقطع ماكان قبله من الخطايا والذنوب،
It is only about belief, so they will be taken to task for abandoning belief only. This issue is not the issue of obligation with belief and performance or belief only; it is preserved and transmitted from the early scholars (Abu Hanifa and his companions). Rather, the people of Bukhara derived it from the statement of Muhammad in al-Mabsut concerning a case: a person who vowed to fast a month then apostatized, that fast was not binding upon him after he believed. So it is known that disbelief invalidates the obligation of performing acts of worship. In our Hanafi fiqh, such issues derived from the statements of the imams are very many.
Evidence of the denier of the obligation of the kafir with the branches:
First: If it were valid, it would be valid from him, due to compliance with the command, and the consequent (validity) is false by agreement. We say: It is refuted by the case of the junub person. The solution is that it is conditional, like the one without wudu.
Second: If compliance were possible, and during disbelief it is not possible, and after it there is no demand. We say: It is possible during disbelief, even if it is not possible under the condition of disbelief, and the conditional impossibility does not negate intrinsic possibility. And it is refuted by faith.
Third: If qada' were obligatory, but it is not obligatory by agreement. We say: The entailment is denied, for Islam wipes out what preceded, so it is as if it is qada' for everything, or it is by a new command.
The deniers argued for the obligation of the kafir with the branches in three ways. Therefore the author said:
-1 (The evidence) for the denier: First, that if obligation of the kafir with the branches were valid, those branches would be valid from him (i.e., their performance would be valid) due to compliance with the command (the command of the Lawgiver), since validity is compliance with the command of the Lawgiver. And the consequent (validity of performing those branches from the kafir) is false by agreement of both deniers and affirmers, because their validity is dependent on faith.
We say in refutation of the evidence of the denier: It is refuted by the case of the junub person, for he is obligated with prayer while in janaba, and his prayer is not valid with janaba (just as the prayer of the kafir is not valid with disbelief). So non-validity of performance does not negate obligation. And the solution from the side of the denier is that the validity of branches from the kafir is only by a condition, which is faith, just as the one without wudu is obligated with prayer under the condition of purification, just as the junub is obligated with prayer under the condition of purification.
2 - And the second evidence for him (the denier): That if the obligation of the kafir with branches were valid, then compliance with those branches would be possible, because possibility is a condition of obligation and is inseparable from it. But compliance from the kafir is not possible, because the possibility of compliance is not free from two states: either it is during disbelief, or it is during Islam and abandoning disbelief. During disbelief, compliance is not possible because niyyah (intention) is necessary for compliance, and the niyyah of a kafir is not considered. Likewise, it is not possible after abandoning disbelief and choosing Islam either, because when a kafir becomes Muslim, he is not required to make up for past obligations, since Islam destroys what came before of sins and bodily and financial obligations. So the command and compliance fall away, for compliance is a branch of the command. We say in response to this evidence: We do not concede that compliance during disbelief is impossible; rather, it is possible during disbelief too, because disbelief is not essential to him, even if it is not possible under the condition of the existence of disbelief. And the impossibility of compliance under the condition of disbelief does not negate intrinsic possibility, i.e., that compliance is possible by essence. This is the explanation of his statement: 'Conditional impossibility does not negate intrinsic possibility.' Its analogy: Every writer necessarily moves his fingers as long as he is a writer. So the moving of fingers for the writer is necessary under the condition of the description of writing, but in itself, the negation of movement of his fingers is possible if the description of writing is not present. And the evidence of the denier is refuted by the obligation of faith, for compliance with the command to have faith during disbelief is not possible, and after faith and the removal of disbelief, the demand for faith and the command to it is a demand for the attainment of what has already been attained.
3 - And the third evidence for him: That if the obligation of the kafir with branches were valid, then those branches would be demanded from the kafir. And if they are demanded, then qada' would be obligatory upon him, i.e., making up the branches after Islam. But qada' is not obligatory upon the kafir after his Islam by agreement, because Islam destroys what came before.
We say in response to this evidence: The entailment (the necessity of qada' upon the kafir due to being obligated with branches) is denied, for Islam obliterates and cuts off what came before of sins and wrongdoings.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi usul dispute concerning whether a disbeliever (kafir) is addressed (mukallaf) with the practical obligations of Sharia (furu', branches). The majority of Hanafis, following Abu Hanifa and his companions, hold that the kafir is indeed obligated with the furu' in the sense that he is ordered to perform them and will be punished for neglecting them, even though his performance is not valid without faith. This passage specifically addresses the arguments of those who deny this obligation (al-nafi). The author first summarizes the three main arguments of the nafi, then provides detailed refutations. The first argument is that if the kafir were obligated, his acts would be valid because they would comply with the command, but validity is impossible without faith. The Hanafi response uses qiyas (analogy) to the state of janaba (major ritual impurity): a junub person is commanded to pray but his prayer is not valid without ghusl; similarly, the kafir is commanded but his acts are not valid without faith. The condition (faith/purification) does not negate the obligation. The second argument is that compliance (imtithal) must be possible for obligation, but the kafir cannot comply: during disbelief his intention is invalid, and after Islam he is not required to make up. The Hanafi response distinguishes between conditional impossibility and intrinsic possibility. The kafir's compliance is intrinsically possible, though conditioned on faith. The argument is also refuted by the obligation of faith itself, which is similarly impossible to comply with during disbelief. The third argument claims that if obligated, qada' (making up missed acts) would be necessary after Islam, but it is not required by consensus. The Hanafi response denies the entailment, citing the principle that Islam wipes out previous obligations. The text ends abruptly before completing the refutation, but the position is clear: the kafir is mukallaf with furu', but the validity of acts and the requirement of qada' are separate issues. This dispute is crucial for understanding the scope of taklif and the basis of punishment for disbelievers in the afterlife for neglecting both belief and practice.

Key Terms
النافي (al-nafi): The one who denies that a kafir is obligated (mukallaf) with the branches (furu') of Sharia.
المثبت (al-muthbit): The one who affirms that a kafir is obligated with the branches.
الإمكان الذاتي (al-imkan al-dhati): Intrinsic possibility; the ability of an act to be performed in itself, irrespective of external conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the denier's second argument that compliance (imtithal) is impossible for a kafir?
A) The kafir can comply by having good intention even while disbelieving.
B) Compliance is intrinsically possible, though conditionally impossible due to kufr; this distinction saves the obligation.
C) The kafir is not obligated at all, so compliance is irrelevant.
D) The obligation of faith is also impossible, so the argument is self-defeating.

Key Takeaway
A kafir is obligated with furu' because the condition of faith does not negate the obligation; compliance is intrinsically possible, and Islam wipes out past obligations so no qada' is needed.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
Taklif al-Kafir bi al-Furu': The Address of the Disbeliever with Subsidiary Obligations
Dispute on whether kafir is addressed by furu' obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on 'taklif al-kafir bi al-furu'' and critically evaluate the arguments of both deniers and affirmers using Quranic evidence and the usul concepts of essential possibility and conditional necessity.

Core Idea
A disbeliever is obligated to obey Islamic branches; the impossibility of compliance in kufr is conditional, not essential.

One of the key usul disputes is whether a disbeliever is addressed by the detailed commands of Shari'ah. The Hanafi school affirms this, while some scholars deny it. This lesson presents the arguments for both sides, the Hanafi responses, and the Quranic proofs that support the majority position. Understanding this dispute clarifies the accountability of disbelievers in the Hereafter and the effect of conversion on past obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The central question is whether a disbeliever is mukallaf (addressed) with the furu' (subsidiary obligations) of Islam, such as prayer, zakat, and Hajj.
2
Analyze the denier's first argument
The denier argues that compliance is impossible for a kafir because intention is invalid, and after Islam, qada' is not required. So the address would be pointless.
3
Examine the Hanafi response: essential vs. conditional possibility
The Hanafi response distinguishes between essential possibility (imkan dhati) and conditional necessity. The kafir's inability is due to the condition of kufr, not an inherent impossibility. Just as a writer's finger movement is necessary only as long as he writes, the non-compliance of a kafir is conditional, not absolute.
Refer to the writer analogy in the commentary.
4
Evaluate the denier's second argument about qada'
The denier claims that if the kafir were obligated, he would have to make up missed acts after Islam, which is not required. The Hanafi response: Islam erases prior obligations, and no new command for qada' has been given.
5
Review the affirmer's Quranic proofs and tarjih
The affirmer cites verses that address 'الناس' (mankind) generally, and verses where disbelievers are punished for neglecting prayer and zakat, proving they were commanded. The author favors this position, stating that interpreting these verses to exclude disbelievers is far-fetched.
Note the tarjih: the author sides with the affirmers.

الجنب مكلفاً بالصلاة بشرط الظهارة.
٢ - والدليل له للنا في ثانياً: أنه لو صح تكليف الكافر بالفروع لأمكن الامتثال بتلك الفروع؛ لأن الإمكان شرط التكليف فلا ينفك عنه، والامتثال من الكافر ليس بممكن؛ لأن إمكان الامتثال لا يخلو عن الحالين إما أن يكون حال الكفر ، أو يكون حال الإسلام وترك الكفر، وفي حال الكفر لا يمكن الامتثال لأنه لابد في الامتثال من النية، ونية الكافر غير معتبرة، وكذلك لا يمكن الامتثال بعد ترك الكفر واختيار الإسلام أيضاً، لأن الكافر إذا أسلم لايُطلب منه قضاء ما مضى من الواجبات ؛ لأن الإسلام يهدم ماكان قبله من الذنوب و الواجبات البدنية والمالية. فسقط عنه الأمر والامتثال فإنّ الامتثال فرع الأمر. قلنا في الجواب عن هذا الدليل : إنا لا نسلّم أن الامتثال حال الكفر غير ممكن ، بل هو ممكن حين الكفر أيضاً لأن ثبوت الكفر له ليس بضرورى ، وإن لم يمكن بشرط وجود الكفر، وضرورة عدم الامتثال بشرط الكفر لاتنا في الإمكان الذاتي ، أي كون الامتثال ممكناً بالذات ، وهذا شرح قوله: "والضرورة الشرطية لا تنافي الإمكان الذاتي نظيره : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بضرورة مادام كاتباً، فثبوت تحرّك الأصابع للكاتب ضرورى بشرط وصف الكتابة، وأما في حد ذاته فسلب تحرك الأصابع عنه ممكن إذالم يكن فيه وصف الكتابة. و ينتقض دليل النافي بالتكليف بالإيمان ، فإنّ الامتثال بالأمر بالإيمان في حال الكفر غير ممكن، و بعد الإيمان وزوال الكفر طلب الايمان والأمر به طلب تحصيل الحاصل. -٣ والدليل له ثالثاً : أنه لوصح تكليف الكافر بالفروع لكانت تلك الفروع مطلوبة من الكافر. وإذا كانت مطلوبة لوجب القضاء عليه، أي قضاء الفروع على الكافر بعد الإسلام، ولا يجب القضاء على الكافر بعد اسلامه اتفاقاً ؛ لأن الإسلام يهدم ما كان قبله. قلنا في الجواب عن هذا الدليل: الملازمة (لزوم القضاء على الكافر لأجل كونه مكلفاً بالفروع ممنوع فإنّ الإسلام يحب ويقطع ماكان قبله من الخطايا والذنوب، فهل هذا (قبول الإسلام ) كأنه قضاء عن كلّ (جميع ) ماترك من الأوامر والنواهي حالة الكفر . أو نقول في الجواب: إن القضاء يجب بأمر جديد، ولم يرد أمر جديد لوجوب القضاء على الكافر بعد إسلامه.

-١ قوله تعالى : كل نفس بما كسبت رهينة إلا أصحاب اليمين ، في جنات يتساءلون عن المجرمين ماسلككم في سقر فيجيبون قالوا لم نك من المصلين ولم نك نطعم المسكين) أى الزكاة ، فعلم أنّ ترك الصلاة والزكاة سلكهم في النار فهم مكلفون بالفروع -٢- وقوله تعالى : (يا ايها الناس اعبدوا ربكم الذي خلقكم ) ونفظ الناس عام للكفار والمؤمنين والمنافقين بإيجاد الإيمان والعبادة، وزيادة العبادة وإيجاد الإخلاص في القلب .
٣- وقوله تعالى : والله على الناس حج البيت من استطاع إليه سبيلاً) فلفظ الناس شامل للكافر والمؤمن فوجب الحج على الكفار أيضاً. إذهب إلى غير ذلك ( غير المذكور) من الآيات -٤- نحو قوله تعالى : (وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مخلصين له الدين حنفاء و يقيم الصلاة ويؤتوا الزكاة ) والمخاطب أهل الكتاب والمشركين - ٥ وقوله تعال والى مدين أخاهم شعيباً قال ياقوم اعبدوالله مالكم من اله غيره وكان مخاطب شعيب (عليه السّلام) غير المؤمنين.
ثم أشار المصنف إلى ترجيح دلائل المثبتين وقال: " والتأويل في الكل " (جميع
The junub is obligated to pray with the condition of purity.
2 - The proof for the denier: second: that if the address of the kafir with the subsidiary branches were valid, then compliance with those branches would be possible; because possibility is a condition of address and does not separate from it, and compliance by the kafir is not possible; because the possibility of compliance is not free from two states: either it is in the state of kufr, or it is in the state of Islam and abandonment of kufr. In the state of kufr, compliance is not possible because intention is necessary for compliance, and the intention of the kafir is not considered. Likewise, compliance is not possible after abandoning kufr and choosing Islam also, because when a kafir becomes Muslim, he is not asked to make up what previously passed of obligations; because Islam destroys what was before it of sins and bodily and financial obligations. So the command and compliance fall away, for compliance is a branch of command. We say in response to this proof: We do not concede that compliance in the state of kufr is not possible, rather it is possible even at the time of kufr because the attribution of kufr to him is not essential, even if it is not possible with the condition of the existence of kufr, and the necessity of non-compliance with the condition of kufr does not negate essential possibility, i.e., that compliance is possible in itself. And this is an explanation of his statement: 'Conditional necessity does not contradict essential possibility. Its analogy: every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, so the existence of finger movement for the writer is necessary with the condition of the attribute of writing, but in itself, the negation of finger movement from him is possible if the attribute of writing is not present.' And the denier's proof is refuted by the address with faith, for compliance with the command of faith in the state of kufr is not possible, and after faith and the removal of kufr, the demand for faith and the command to it is a demand to obtain what has already been obtained.
3 - His proof: third: that if the address of the kafir with the subsidiary branches were valid, then those branches would be required of the kafir. And if they are required, then making them up would be obligatory upon him, i.e., making up the branches after Islam, and making up after Islam is not obligatory upon the kafir by consensus; because Islam destroys what was before it. We say in response to this proof: The necessary consequence (the obligation of making up for the kafir because of being addressed with the branches) is denied, for Islam removes and cuts off what was before it of mistakes and sins, so this (accepting Islam) is as if it is a substitute for all that was left of commands and prohibitions in the state of kufr. Or we say in the response: Making up is obligatory by a new command, and no new command has come for the obligation of making up for the kafir after his Islam.
Proofs of the affirmers of the address of the kafir with the branches
And for the affirmer are the verses: 'We were not of the prayerful nor did we feed the poor' i.e., zakat, and 'O people, worship your Lord who created you', and 'And for God upon the people is the Hajj of the House', etc., and the interpretation of all is far-fetched.
And the proofs of the affirmer are the following verses:
1 - His statement, Exalted: 'Every soul is a pledge for what it has earned, except the companions of the right hand, in gardens they will ask about the criminals: What led you into Saqar? They will say: We were not of those who prayed, nor did we feed the poor' i.e., zakat. So it is known that leaving prayer and zakat led them into the Fire, so they are addressed with the branches.
2 - His statement, Exalted: 'O people, worship your Lord who created you' and the word 'people' is general for disbelievers, believers, and hypocrites, requiring the establishment of faith and worship, the increase of worship, and the establishment of sincerity in the heart.
3 - His statement, Exalted: 'And for God upon the people is the Hajj of the House, for whoever is able to find a way there' so the word 'people' includes the disbeliever and the believer, so Hajj is obligatory upon disbelievers as well. Go to other (than mentioned) verses.
4 - Such as His statement, Exalted: 'And they were not commanded except to worship God, being sincere to Him in religion, upright, and to establish prayer and give zakat' and the addressees are the People of the Book and the polytheists.
5 - His statement, Exalted: 'And to Madyan their brother Shu'ayb, he said: O my people, worship God, you have no god other than Him' and the addressees of Shu'ayb (peace be upon him) were the non-believers.
Then the author indicated the tarjih of the proofs of the affirmers and said: 'And interpretation (of all the verses used by the deniers) is far-fetched.'
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage addresses a fundamental usul dispute: whether a disbeliever (kafir) is addressed and obligated by the detailed commands of the Shari'ah (furu') while persisting in kufr. The author presents the arguments of the deniers (al-nafi) and then the proponents (al-muthbit), ultimately favoring the proponents. The deniers raise two main proofs: first, that compliance is impossible in the state of kufr because intention is required and the kafir's intention is invalid; second, that if the kafir were obligated, he would have to make up missed acts after Islam, which is not required by consensus. The author refutes these by distinguishing essential possibility (imkan dhati) from conditional impossibility—the kafir's inability is due to his chosen state, not to an inherent deficiency in the act itself. He also points out that the denier's argument would also invalidate the obligation of faith (iman) itself. Regarding qada' (making up), the author notes that Islam wipes out previous obligations, so no new command for qada' has been revealed. The proponents then cite several Quranic verses where the address 'O people' or 'O mankind' includes disbelievers, and where disbelievers are punished for neglecting prayer and zakat, proving that they were indeed commanded. The author concludes by stating that interpreting these verses to exclude disbelievers is far-fetched (al-ta'wil fi al-kull ba'id), thus siding with the position that kafirs are addressed with the subsidiary branches.

Key Terms
تكليف الكافر بالفروع (taklīf al-kāfir bi al-furūʿ): The legal doctrine that a disbeliever is addressed and obliged by the detailed commands of the Shari'ah (subsidiary branches) even while in a state of kufr.
الإمكان الذاتي (al-imkān al-dhātī): Essential possibility: the inherent capacity of an action to be performed, independent of external conditions. In this context, the act of compliance is possible in itself even if the kafir's current state prevents it.
الضرورة الشرطية (al-ḍarūra al-sharṭiyya): Conditional necessity: a necessity that arises from a specific condition (e.g., the writer's finger movement is necessary as long as he is writing). It does not negate the essential possibility of the opposite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Hanafi response to the denier's argument that compliance is impossible for a kafir?
A) The kafir can intend sincerely by recognizing the truth.
B) The impossibility is conditional, not essential; the act is possible in itself.
C) The kafir is not required to have intention for acts of worship.
D) The denier's argument contradicts the consensus on the obligation of faith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that disbelievers are addressed with subsidiary obligations (furūʿ) based on general Qur'anic commands, and the impossibility of compliance in kufr is conditional, not essential; conversion to Islam erases prior obligations.

Obligation Attaches Only to Acts, Not Non-Existence
Defining that taklif attaches to acts, not to mere absence of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that taklif (obligation) attaches only to acts (af‘āl) and identify the rational basis for this view, including the distinction between refraining as an act and mere non-existence.

Core Idea
Taklif attaches to acts – even refraining from a sin is an act of will, not mere passivity.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh: the scope of obligation (taklīf). The Ḥanafīs hold that obligation only relates to acts (af‘āl), not to mere non-existence (ʿadam). This differs from many Muʿtazila who consider both act and non-act as directly commanded or prohibited. Understanding this distinction is critical for grasping how compliance with prohibitions is achieved and how human power (qudra) is defined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the opening statement: 'Lā taklīfa illā bi al-fiʿl' (there is no obligation except for the act). This is the thesis. Note the exception: opposing view of 'many Muʿtazila'.
2
Analyze the dispute over prohibitions
The passage clarifies that the dispute is not about mere non-existence due to absence of will (no one disputes that), but about non-existence that results from the will (i.e., when one wills to refrain). The Hanafis argue that the object of will is not the non-existence itself but the means: abstention (kaff) and resolve (ʿazm).
3
Examine the rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī)
The Hanafi proof: will (mashīʾa) attaches only to existents; non-existence as such is pure nothing. Therefore, obligation cannot directly command non-existence; it commands the act of refraining which is a real, existent act of will.
This is a purely rational argument, not dependent on textual sources.
4
Understand the definition of power (qudra)
The Hanafis define human power as: 'If he wills he does, if he wills he leaves' – not 'if he wills he does not do' or 'if he does not will he does not do'. This emphasizes that leaving is an act (tark is a fiʿl) not a passive absence.
5
Address the case of heedlessness (ghafla)
The text anticipates an objection: what if someone is heedless of a prohibition? The Hanafi response: there is no taklif for the heedless. Once aware, one must form a resolve (ʿazm) to abstain; otherwise punishment applies. This reinforces that compliance requires an intentional act.
The passage ends abruptly; the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فنحن نقول: لا يتعلق به المشية بالذات؛ لأنها تقتضي الشيئية (الوجود) والعدم من حيث هو عدم لاشيئ محض، فلا سبيل إليه إلا بتعلقها بماهو وسيلة إليه، وهو الكف عنه، والعزم على الترك، وهو معنى مقدورية العدم، وإن أثر المشية الاستمرار، وإلا فالعدم أصل واستمراره باستمرار عدم علة الوجود، لا بالقدرة، ولهذا عرفوها بـ"إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك" دون "إن شاء لم يفعل" أو "إن لم يشأ لم يفعل"، قيل فحين الغفلة يلزم فوت الواجب، وهو الكف فيعاقب قلنا: لا تكليف للغافل، وبعد الشعور يجب العزم، وإلا يعاقب بناء
4. Issue: That obligation is only for the act, or for it and its nonexistence. There is no obligation except for the act, contrary to many of the Mu'tazila. And it is regarding the prohibition (nahy) itself. There is no dispute about the nonexistence of the act due to the absence of will, for the cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause of existence. Rather [the dispute is] about the nonexistence of the act for [the presence of] will, and this is what compliance in prohibition is realized by, and reward is attached to it. So we say: will does not attach to it (nonexistence) essentially; because will requires thingness (existence), and nonexistence insofar as it is nonexistence is pure nothing, so there is no way to it except by its attachment to what is a means to it, which is abstention from it (al-kaff 'anhu) and resolve upon abandonment (al-'azm 'ala al-tark), and that is the meaning of the power (maqduriyyah) of non-existence. And the effect of will is continuance (al-istimrar), otherwise non-existence is original and its continuance is by the continuance of the absence of the cause of existence, not by power. For this reason they defined it as: 'If he wills he does, and if he wills he leaves,' not 'if he wills he does not do' or 'if he does not will he does not do.' It was said: then at the time of heedlessness (ghaflah) the loss of the obligatory act occurs, which is abstention (kaff), so he would be punished. Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage provides a nuanced Hanafi definition of the scope of obligation (taklif). The author states that taklif only applies to positive acts (fi'l), not to pure non-existence ('adm). This is in contrast to many Mu'tazila who considered both act and non-act as directly subject to obligation. For prohibitions (nahy), the required compliance is abstaining, but that abstaining is itself an act—specifically the act of refraining (kaff) and resolving to avoid (azm). The author argues that non-existence per se is not directly willable; we cannot will nothingness. Instead, we will the means to achieve non-existence: the act of holding back. Human power (qudra) is defined as 'if he wills he does, if he wills he leaves'—not 'if he wills he does not do,' because non-doing is the default state. The natural state is non-existence; it continues as long as the cause of existence is absent, not through our power. The final segment addresses the case of heedlessness: if one forgets a prohibition, there is no sin because there is no taklif for the unaware. But upon remembering, one must form a resolution (azm) to desist; otherwise, punishment applies. This is a typical Hanafi usul analysis emphasizing that obligations are attached to acts within human capacity, and that abstention is an act of will, not mere passivity.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklīf): Obligation; the imposition of a legal demand that involves a burden, applicable only to acts within human capacity.
الكف (al-kaff): Abstention or refraining; an act of the will that is the means to avoid a prohibited act.
العزم (al-‘azm): Resolve or firm intention to abstain; required for compliance with prohibitions after awareness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'If he wills he does, and if he does not will he does not'
C) 'If he wills he does, if he wills he leaves'
D) 'He has power over both act and non-act equally'

Key Takeaway
Obligation only concerns acts within human power; even refraining from sin is a positive act of will.

Taklif Only for Actions: The Hanafi View on Obligation and Non-Existence
Argues that legal obligation only pertains to actions, not omissions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that taklif (obligation) only pertains to actions (fi'l) and not to mere non-existence (adam), and identify the proof from the rational analysis of will (mashi'a).

Core Idea
Obligation requires action; even refraining from the forbidden is an action, not inaction.

This passage from the Usul text tackles a foundational dispute about the nature of legal obligation. The Hanafi school, following Ahl al-Sunna, insists that obligation can only be directed at an existent act. Against the Mu'tazila, who argue that prohibitions directly command non-existence, the Hanafis analyze the relationship between divine will and the objects of obligation. Understanding this ruling clarifies why compliance with prohibitions is an act of refraining (kaf), not a mere absence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the opposing view
State the Hanafi position: taklif is only for actions. Note the Mu'tazila claim that in prohibitions, the obligation is directly for non-existence.
The text begins with 'لا تكليف إلا بالفعل' (no obligation except for action).
2
Analyze the concept of non-existence (adam)
Distinguish two types of non-existence: (1) due to lack of cause (e.g., no will) – not a valid object of obligation; (2) due to will (choosing not to act) – the obligation is actually on the act of refraining (kaf).
The text says: 'النزاع في عدم الفعل لأجل المشيئة' (the dispute is about non-existence due to will).
3
Trace the proof: will only attaches to existents
The Hanafi proof is rational: divine will (mashi'a) necessitates thingness (shay'iyya) and existence. Pure non-existence cannot be intended. Therefore, commands and prohibitions must target actions.
Key phrase: 'المشيئة تقتضي الشيئية والوجود' (the will necessitates thingness and existence).
4
Examine the definition of ability (qudra)
The standard definition of ability is 'if he wills he does, if he wills he leaves' (إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك), not 'if he wills he does not do' (إن شاء لم يفعل). This shows that the object of ability is always an action.
This definition is from the source: 'ولهذا عرفوها بـ إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك دون إن شاء لم يفعل'.
5
Consider the objection about heedlessness (ghafla) and conclusion
The objection: if someone sleeps through prayer time, they are sinful for the non-existence of prayer. Hanafi response: the sin is for failing to take means (an action), and obligation does not apply during heedlessness. The final summary: compliance is only by what is possible (maqdur), which is action in commands and refraining in prohibitions.
The text ends with: 'أن الامتثال لا يكون إلا بالمقدور، وهو الفعل في الأمر والكف في النهي'.

Issue: Whether obligation is only for action or for it and its opposite. There is no obligation except for action, contrary to many Mu'tazila. And this non-existence is not suitable to be the basis of obligation. So there is no dispute about the unsuitability of this non-existence for being attached to obligation. Rather, the dispute is about non-existence of action due to will, i.e., the agent did not will the action, so he did not do it, or he willed its abandonment, so it remained in its non-existence. This non-existence due to will is the non-existence by which compliance in prohibition is realized, and reward is consequent upon this compliance. So we say: Non-existence is not a locus for obligation, because the will does not essentially attach to it (non-existence); because the will necessitates thingness and existence. Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the nature of taklif (legal obligation). The Hanafi position, following Ahl al-Sunna, is that obligation only pertains to actions (fi'l), not to mere inactions (adam). The Mu'tazila argue that in prohibitions, the obligation is essentially to refrain, which they consider an action of the soul. The author refutes this by analyzing the concept of 'adam' (non-existence). He distinguishes between non-existence due to lack of cause (which is not an object of will or obligation) and non-existence due to will (where one chooses not to act). Even in the latter case, the obligation is not directed at the non-existence itself but at the act of refraining (kaf), which is an existent. Thus, compliance in prohibition is achieved by the act of refraining, not by mere non-existence. The discussion clarifies that will cannot attach to pure non-existence; it only attaches to existents. Therefore, commands are for actions, and prohibitions are for the act of refraining (which is also an action). The madhhab dispute includes a rational proof and responses to objections.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklif): Legal obligation imposed by the Lawgiver; according to the Hanafis, it pertains only to actions (fi'l) that are possible.
عدم (adam): Non-existence; pure non-being that cannot be an object of will or obligation because it has no reality.
كف (kaf): Refraining; an act of the soul (or body) that is existent and thus a valid object of obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Taklif (obligation) only applies to existent actions; in prohibitions, the commanded act is the act of refraining (kaf), which is an existent act, not a mere absence.

Can Non-Action Be Commanded? The Hanafi View on Taklif and Prohibitions
Analyzing whether non-action can be an object of taklif and its relation to ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether non-action (adam) can be the object of taklif and explain the role of refraining (kaf) as an existential act that is the true object of obligation in prohibitions.

Core Idea
Non-action cannot be directly commanded; what is commanded is the existential act of refraining (kaf).

This lesson delves into a foundational dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: can the Lawgiver command non-action? At first glance, prohibitions like 'Do not commit zina' seem to order us not to do something. But is the obligation to simply not do it, or is there a positive act required? The Hanafi school argues that non-action per se is not within human capability (qudrah) because volition only attaches to existent acts. Instead, the object of obligation in a prohibition is the existential act of refraining (kaf), which is a volitional restraint of the self. This lesson unpacks the rational proof, the opposing Mu'tazili view, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage first frames the dispute: Can non-action (adam) be the object of taklif (obligation)? The Hanafi answer is no, because volition does not attach to pure non-existence. The Mu'tazila hold that in prohibitions, the obligation is directly about non-action.
The dispute is not about whether non-action that results from the absence of volition can be obligated – that is agreed to be impossible. The dispute is about non-action that accompanies volition (e.g., willing not to do the act).
2
State the Hanafi position and its rational proof
The Hanafi position is that compliance (imtithal) in a prohibition is through refraining (kaf), which is an existential act (fi'l wujudi). The proof: capability (qudrah) is defined as 'if he wills, he does; if he wills, he leaves.' The 'leaving' here is not non-action but the act of refraining, which is a positive mental act. Volition (mashi'ah) only attaches to existent things, so it cannot attach to non-action itself.
The author emphasizes that the capable act (maqdur) in a prohibition is the kaf, not the resulting non-action.
3
Identify the opposing Mu'tazili view
Many Mu'tazila hold that the obligation in a prohibition is directly about non-action. For example, the command 'Do not move your tongue' is taken as a command to bring about non-movement, not to perform an act of restraint.
The Mu'tazila consider the mere non-action sufficient for compliance; they reward/punish for the non-action itself.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the linguistic form of a prohibition (e.g., 'Do not do X') is understood as 'Restrain yourself from X,' which mandates an existential act (kaf). This is because volition cannot attach to pure non-existence; it attaches to the means to non-existence, which is the act of refraining. The passage also addresses the objection from heedlessness (ghafla): if refraining is mandatory, then a person who is heedless of the prohibition would sin. The response is that there is no obligation during heedlessness; obligation begins only when one is aware.
The author uses the example of a person invited to zina who is praised not for the non-action but for the act of refraining.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference lies in when a person is considered compliant and rewarded. For the Hanafis, reward is for the act of refraining; for the Mu'tazila, reward could be for the mere non-action. This affects how we understand the responsibility of a person who is unable to perform the kaf (e.g., unconscious) or who refrains without conscious will.
The passage also shows that the definition of capability (qudrah) itself is shaped by this dispute.

Removal of the Terror from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut: On the non-capable. The summary is that compliance (imtithal) is only through what is capable (maqdur), which is the act in the case of a command and refraining (kaf) in the case of a prohibition. As for non-compliance, it occurs either due to lack of capability, as in abandoning an obligatory act, or due to doing what is capable, as in committing a forbidden act. As for non-action (adam) that is inherently capable (maqdur bi al-dhat), it is pure non-action; it has no entry into anything. So what is said is not rebutted: if non-action of the act were not capable, then sin in abandoning the obligatory would not be entailed except by refraining from it. For the necessary connection is denied; indeed, sin may occur due to lack of the capable even if the non-action is not capable. They said: Whoever is invited to fornication and does not do it is praised without the act of the opposite crossing his mind. We said: This is denied; rather, it is due to refraining from it. This is: There is no obligation (according to Ahl al-Sunnah) except by bringing the act into existence and performing it. [Page 261] Contrary to many Mu'tazila. And that is: obligation (taklif) according to them occurs in the prohibition, and what is meant is obligation by its necessary concomitant (lazim), which is restraining one's self from the prohibited thing. And refraining (kaf) is an existential act. So when it is said: 'Do not move your tongue,' its meaning is 'Be silent.' The basis of the dispute in this issue rests on two matters: First: whether non-action (adam) is suitable for being the object of obligation or not. Second: whether non-action is capable (maqdur) or not. So the author (musannif) detailed the dispute and said: There is no dispute among anyone that the non-action of an act due to the absence of volition (mashi'ah of the agent) – for the cause of non-action (the non-existence of something) is the absence of the cause of its existence, and volition is among the causes of existence, so its absence is a cause for the absence of existence – this non-action is not suitable to be the locus (manat) of obligation. So there is no dispute regarding the unsuitability of this non-action for obligation attaching to it. Rather, the dispute is about non-action of the act due to volition, i.e., the agent did not will the act, so he did not do it, or he willed to leave it, so he remained in his state of non-action. And that is the non-action due to volition; it is the non-action by which compliance in the prohibition is realized, and upon this compliance rewards are entailed in the case of a command. So we say: Non-action is not a locus for obligation, because volition does not attach to it (non-action) by itself; for volition requires thingness and existence. And non-action (non-action of the act considered in itself, i.e., in that non-action is non-action and remains in its state of non-existence, pure nothingness, i.e., pure non-existent, having no existence in the external world nor in the mind, so there is no path to non-action) in the attachment of volition to it by any means except by the attachment of volition to what is a means to it (to non-action of the act), which is refraining (kaf) from it (from the forbidden act) and resolve (azm) to leave it. That is, if the obligated person wills to restrain himself from it and resolves to leave it. And that (the refraining and resolve of leaving) is the meaning of the capability (maqduriyya) of non-action of the act, i.e., the obligated person is capable by means of that which is a means to non-action of the act, and that means is the restraining of the self from it (i.e., from that act) and the resolve to leave it. And that (the refraining and resolve of leaving) is the meaning of his statement: 'The effect of volition is the continuation of the means of leaving the act and its non-action, which is restraining the self from it and the resolve to leave it.' Otherwise, i.e., if the meaning is not as mentioned, then the statement is not valid; for non-action is the original non-action (of the act) to which volition does not attach, and the continuation of non-action of the act is only through the continuation of the absence of the cause of existence, not by capability over it. Rather, capability is over that which is a means to non-action of the act, as has passed. The correct definition of capability (qudrah): The author said: For this reason, i.e., because non-action (of the act) only occurs due to the absence of volition of existence, and because volition attaches to refraining from that non-existent act, they defined capability as 'If its possessor wills, he does; and if he wills, he leaves.' And they did not say in the second part of the definition: 'If he wills, he does not do.' Or they defined it as 'If he wills, he does; and if he does not will, he does not do.' In the first, they did not attribute non-action to volition; in the second, they attributed non-action to the absence of volition. It was said (in rebuttal to refraining being required in obligation by prohibition): If refraining were required in the prohibition, then at the time of heedlessness (heedlessness of the obligated person) of the forbidden act, i.e., he does not pay attention to it, then the obligatory act (which is refraining by choice) would be missed, and during heedlessness the refraining is involuntary, so he would be punished or blamed for abandoning the obligatory, while that is not the case according to anyone. We said: There is no obligation for the heedless person at the time of heedlessness; he is not obligated at that time, so there is no obligation, no punishment, and no blame. After awareness and realization of the prohibition of the act, it becomes obligatory to resolve to leave it and refrain from it; otherwise he is punished for leaving it based on his not performing the capable act, which is refraining and resolve to leave.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi usul analysis of the nature of obligation (taklif) as it relates to commanded acts and prohibited acts. The author opens by stating that compliance (imtithal) is only possible through something within one's capability (maqdur): for commands, it is the act itself; for prohibitions, it is refraining (kaf). Non-compliance can result from either incapacity (as in abandoning a wajib) or from performing a prohibited act. The key issue is whether non-action (adam) itself can be the object of obligation, as some Mu'tazila claim. The Hanafi position (Ahl al-Sunnah) is that obligation only attaches to the production of an act; for prohibitions, the obligation is to refrain, but refraining is an existential act (kaf) which is a volitional restraint of the self. The dispute is parsed into two questions: Can non-action be an object of obligation? Is non-action within human capability? The author explains that there is agreement that non-action resulting from absence of volition (i.e., when the agent simply does not will the act) cannot be a locus for obligation because volition only attaches to things that exist. However, the real dispute concerns non-action that accompanies volition: when the agent wills not to do the act, that willing is actually a volition to refrain. Thus, the capable act is the refraining itself, not the non-action. The Hanafis define capability (qudrah) as 'if he wills, he does; if he wills, he leaves' — the second clause refers to the power to leave, which is the power to perform the refraining act, not the power to bring about non-action directly. The passage then addresses an objection about heedlessness (ghaflah): if refraining is required, then during heedlessness one would be sinning for not refraining, which is absurd. The response is that the heedless person is not obligated at that moment; obligation only begins when one is aware of the prohibition, at which point one must resolve and refrain. This is a sophisticated discussion of the ontology of obligation and the nature of human acts in Usul al-Fiqh.

Key Terms
امتثال (imtithal): Compliance with a command or prohibition by performing the required act or refraining.
عدم مقدور (adam maqdur): Non-action that is within capacity, i.e., the state of not doing an act when one has the ability to do it.
الكف (al-kaf): The existential act of restraining oneself from a prohibited act; it is the means to non-action and is itself a volitional act.
المشيئة (al-mashi'ah): Volition or will, which is a cause of existence; it attaches only to existent things or to means leading to existence.
القدرة (al-qudrah): Capability defined as 'if the possessor wills, he does; if he wills, he leaves' – the power to perform an act or its opposite (refraining).
العزم (al-'azm): Resolve; a firm intention to perform or refrain from an act. In the passage, 'azm is part of the means to non-action along with kaf.
الغفلة (al-ghafla): Heedlessness; lack of attention or awareness. The passage uses it to discuss the timing of obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the object of obligation in the case of a prohibition (nahy)?
A) The non-action (adam) of the prohibited act
B) The refraining (kaf) which is an existential act of restraint
C) The absence of volition (mashi'ah) for the act
D) The mere thought of not doing the act

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the object of obligation in prohibitions is the existential act of refraining (kaf), not the mere non-action, because volition only attaches to existent acts and capability is defined as power to act or to leave (where 'leave' means the act of refraining).

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Compliance, Reward, and the Mu'tazila Argument on Non-Action
Usul dispute on compliance, reward, and non-action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between compliance through an act of refraining versus mere non-existence, and evaluate the Mu'tazila argument that obligation attaches to non-action.

Core Idea
Compliance requires an act of refraining, not mere non-existence; original non-existence carries no reward or punishment.

The Hanafi school meticulously defines compliance (imtithal) and its relation to human capacity. This passage presents a core usul dispute: does obligation (taklif) attach to non-action as well as action? The Mu'tazila argue yes, using the case of a person invited to adultery who is praised for not committing it. The Hanafi response clarifies that the praise is for the positive act of refraining, not for the non-action itself. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how the Hanafi school derives rulings on prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi framework for compliance and non-compliance
Read the first part of the passage to understand that compliance resulting in reward requires an act within one's power: action for commands, refraining for prohibitions. Non-compliance punishable by sin occurs either by failing to perform a commanded act or by performing a prohibited act.
Focus on the terms 'imtithal' and 'adam al-asli'.
2
Understand the concept of original non-existence
The passage distinguishes between the non-existence of an act that is within one's power (e.g., not doing a wajib) and the original non-existence (al-‘adam al-asli) which has no connection to reward or punishment. This is a key Hanafi principle: what is not within one's power cannot be subject to obligation.
Example: the inherent non-existence of an act one never intended is not blameworthy.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila proof
The Mu'tazila argue that a person invited to adultery who does not commit it is praised for the non-action itself, even without intending the opposite. They conclude that non-action can be obligatory and thus obligation attaches to non-action as well.
The specific case: da'ā ilā al-zinā fa-lam yaf‘al.
4
Follow the Hanafi refutation
The Hanafi response denies that the praise is for non-action; instead, it is for the positive act of refraining (kaff) and avoidance (ijtināb). Thus obligation attaches to the act of refraining, not to mere non-existence.
The passage uses 'kaff' and 'ijtināb' to emphasize the positive nature of the act.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: The Mu'tazila reward mere non-performance of a prohibited act, while Hanafis require a conscious act of refraining for reward. This affects how one fulfills obligations and receives divine reward.
Consider: does a person who sleeps through a prohibition receive reward? Hanafi: no, because no act of refraining occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل (حاصل البحث) أن الامتثال الذى يترتب عليه الثواب، لا يكون إلا بإتيان المقدور ، وهو الفعل في التكليف بالأمر، والكف في التكليف بالنهى وأما عدم الامتثال الموجب للعقاب فيكون تارة لعدم الإتيان بالفعل المقدور، كما في ترك الواجب ، ويكون لعدم الامتثال بالفعل المقدور ، كما في إتيان الفعل الحرام الذى نُهى عنه ، وأما عدم الفعل المقدور للعبد عدماً ذاتياً (سواء كان خيراً أو شراً) فيكون لأجل عدم صدور ذلك الفعل في ذاته، ولا دخل لهذا العدم في شيئ من الثواب والعقاب ولا الامتثال وعدمه، فإنّه عدم أصلى و ذاتى لا يترتب عليه شيئ، وقد قيل في حاشية شرح المختصر (مختصر ابن الحاجب للفاضل ميرزاجان: لولم يكن عدم إتيان الفعل الواجب مقدوراً لم يترتب الإثم في ترك الواجب وعدم إتيانه إلا بالكف عنه ( عن الواجب) وكفّه ليس بمقدور له والتالى باطل ، ووجه لزوم عدم ترتب الإثم أن المؤاخذة بما ليس في قدرته باطل، فأشار إلى دفعه، وقال: لأن الملازمة بين عدم مقدورية ترك الفعل وبين ترتب الإثم بترك الواجب ممنوعة ، فإن الإثم قديكون لعدم إتيان المقدور إذا كان واجباً، وإن لم يكن عدم الإتيان في نفسه مقدوراً، فلهذا قال المصنف في اول الرد" فلايرد ماقيل. دليل المعتزلة على أن التكليف يكون في الفعل وعدمه، ورده قالوا المعتزلة من دُعِيَ إلى الزنا فلم يفعل (الزنا) يُمدح، على عدم الفعل من غير أن يخطر بباله ضد الزنا وهو نهي النفس عن الفاحشة ، فالممدوح هو الواجب أي عدم فعل الزنا، فالعدم في النهي ( ولا تقربوا الزنا واجب، فبطل القول بأن لا يتعلق التكليف إلا بالفعل ". قلنا في جواب المعتزلة : بأنا لانسلم أن المدعوّ يمدح بعدم الفعل وتركه، فإن الممدوح على عدم الفعل ممنوع بل يكون المدح للكف والاجتناب عن الزنا، خذ هذا.
And the summary of the inquiry is that compliance which results in reward only occurs by performing what is within one's power—that is, action in the case of a command, and refraining in the case of a prohibition. As for non-compliance that necessitates punishment, it sometimes occurs due to not performing an act within one's power, as in abandoning an obligatory act, and sometimes due to non-compliance with an act within one's power, as in committing a forbidden act that was prohibited. As for the non-existence of an act within the servant's power as a non-existence inherent (whether good or evil), it is due to the act not occurring in itself, and this non-existence has no connection to reward or punishment, nor to compliance or its absence. It is an original and essential non-existence on which nothing is based. It was said in the gloss of the commentary on al-Mukhtasar (Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib by al-Fadil Mirzajan): If the non-performance of an obligatory act were not within one's power, then sin would not follow from abandoning it unless by refraining from it, and refraining is not within one's power—the consequent is false. The reason for the necessary lack of sin is that taking to account for what is not in one's power is invalid. He indicated its refutation and said: The entailment between the lack of power over leaving the act and the sin of leaving it is denied. For sin may be due to not performing what is within one's power when it is obligatory, even if the non-performance itself is not within one's power. Hence the author said at the beginning of the response: "Thus what was said is not rebutted." The proof of the Mu'tazila that obligation applies to both action and non-action, and its refutation: They said: A person who is called to adultery and does not commit it is praised for the non-action, without any thought of the opposite of adultery, i.e., refraining from indecency. So what is praised is the obligatory, i.e., the non-doing of adultery. Thus non-action in the prohibition ("Do not approach adultery") is obligatory, so the statement that obligation only attaches to action is invalid. We respond to the Mu'tazila: We do not concede that the called person is praised for non-action and leaving it. Rather, the praise for non-action is rejected; instead, praise is for the refraining and avoidance of adultery. Take this.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute addressing two key issues: (1) the nature of compliance (imtithal) and its relation to reward and punishment, and (2) a refutation of the Mu'tazila argument that obligation applies to non-action. The Hanafi position is that compliance leading to reward requires performing what is within one's power—either acting (for commands) or refraining (for prohibitions). Non-compliance punishable by sin occurs either by failing to perform a commanded act (e.g., leaving a wajib) or by performing a prohibited act. However, the mere non-existence of an act (the absence of something) is not within human power and carries no moral valuation; it is 'original' non-existence. The text then engages a gloss from Mirzajan: if omitting an obligatory act were not within one's power, then sin would only follow from refraining from it, but refraining is also not in one's power—clearly false. The author rejects this entailment, arguing that sin results from not performing a commanded act that is within one's power, even if the non-performance itself is not volitional. Next, the Mu'tazila present their proof: a person invited to adultery who does not commit it is praised for not doing it, even without intending the opposite—so non-action can be obligatory. The Hanafi response: such a person is not praised for the non-action per se, but for the positive act of refraining (kaff) and avoidance (ijtinab). Thus obligation attaches to the act of refraining, not to mere non-existence. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi precision in distinguishing between commanded acts (including the act of refraining) and morally neutral non-existence.

Key Terms
الامتثال (al-imtithal): Compliance with a command or prohibition by performing the required action (for commands) or refraining (for prohibitions), resulting in reward.
العدم الأصلي (al-‘adam al-asli): Original non-existence: the state of an act not occurring inherently, without any human causation; it carries no moral or legal consequence.
الكف (al-kaff): The act of refraining or withholding oneself from a prohibited act; a positive volitional act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, why is a person who is invited to adultery and does not commit it praised?
A) Because he did not commit the act of adultery, which is a non-action.
B) Because he performed the act of refraining (kaff) and avoidance.
C) Because he had no intention to commit adultery.
D) Because non-action is itself an act of obedience.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul holds that compliance requires a positive act (action or refraining); original non-existence merits no reward or punishment.

10 min
The Timing of Obligation: Taklif Before the Act
Refuting the attribution to al-Ash'ari that there is no obligation before the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school insists that taklif must precede the act and refute the attributed Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Taklif must precede the act; otherwise, compliance is impossible and disbelievers would not be obligated.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute: does legal obligation (taklif) exist before an act or concurrent with it? The author of our text, al-Badakhshani, strongly refutes the view attributed to Imam al-Ash'ari that there is no taklif before the act, calling it necessarily false. We will unpack the rational arguments, understand the consequences, and see why the Hanafi position is essential for accountability.

Guided Path
1
State the disputed issue
Identify the central question: Is the obligation to perform an act located before the act occurs, or does it arise simultaneously with the act?
This is a classical usul al-fiqh dispute with significant implications for taklif and compliance.
2
Present the attributed Ash'ari view
Note the claim attributed to al-Ash'ari: 'la taklif qabla al-fi'l' (no obligation before the act). The author immediately labels this as necessarily false.
The attribution itself is debated, but the refutation stands on rational grounds.
3
Understand the Hanafi refutation
The author points to two necessary consequences: (1) the disbeliever would not be obligated to believe before actually believing, and (2) compliance (imtithal) would be impossible because it requires prior knowledge of the obligation.
Compliance is defined as choosing the act after knowing the obligation, which presupposes prior taklif.
4
Examine the detailed rebuttal
The text then addresses a more nuanced argument: that the act is within power as an effect of power, so taklif concurrent with the act is valid. The author refutes this by denying that power has any effect (in the Ash'ari framework), and if conceded, it does not entail being within power. Further, for extended acts, the demand can be analysed into parts, each part preceding the corresponding act part, and demanding what already exists is absurd.
This demonstrates the logical rigor of the Hanafi approach.
5
Conclude with the thamarah al-khilaf
If taklif were not before the act, one could not be held accountable for failing to do something before the command exists—undermining the entire system of obligation. The author cites Imam al-Haramayn's strong rejection.
This practical consequence shows why the issue matters for the validity of Shari'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة أن التكليف يكون قبل الفعل أو معه؟ نسب إلى الأشعرى أن لا تكليف قبل الفعل، وهو غلط بالضرورة، كيف لا؟ ويلزم نفي تكليف الكافر بالإيمان، ونفي الامتثال، فإنه باختيار الفعل بعد العلم بالتكليف، ومع ذلك قد تبعه جماعة، منهم صاحب المنهاج ، ولله در الإمام الحرمين، حيث قال : هذا مذهب لا يرتضيه العاقل لنفسه، وفي "الإحكام التكليف ثابت قبله وينقطع اتفاقاً، وهل هو باق على حال حدوثه؟ قال به الأشعرى وهو باطل؛ لأنه كما تقول: الطلب باق حين وجود المطلوب، وهو كما ترى وما يقال: إن التكليف متعلق بالمجموع، وهو يحدث شيئاً فشيئاً، فيلزم مقارنته بالحدوث، فمع أنه لايتم في الأنيات فاسد؛ لأن الفعل إذا كان ممتداً كان الطلب المتعلق به محللاً إلى الأجزاء، فكل جزء منه مسبوق بجزء من الطلب، قالوا: الفعل مقدور حيثنذ؛ لأنه أثر القدرة، فيصح التكليف به، إذ لا مانع إلا عدم القدرة، وقد انتفى قلنا: لا نسلم أنه أثرها؛ فإنه لا تاثير للقدرة عندكم؛ ولوسلم فلانسلم أنه يستلزم المقدورية، فإنه يجب بالاختيار؛ لأن الشئ مالم يجب لم يوجد، ولوسلم فلانسلم أن لا مانع إلا ذلك، بل لزوم طلب الموجود ونُسب إلى الأشعرى أن لا تكليف قبل الفعل ، وهو كون هذا المذهب منسوباً إلى الأشعرى ( غلط بالضرورة والبداهة ، ثم تبه على كونه غلطاً وقال: كيف لا تكون هذه النسبة خطأ؟ ويلزم من هذا المذهب أو هذا القول أمران : الأول: أنه على هذا التقدير لا يكون العبد مكلفاً قبل قبول الإيمان وفعله. والثاني : يلزم منه نفي الامتثال ، فإنّ الامتثال إنما يتحقق باختيار الفعل و مباشرته بعدا لعلم بالتكليف ، وشعور كونه مكلفاً، ولا تكليف في هذه المسألة قبل الفعل حتى يعلمه . الخطأ) ثم أشار إلى خطأ جماعة من العلماء وقال : ومع ذلك (أي مع هذا الانتساب تبع الأشعرى جماعة من العلماء) منهم الإمام البيضاوي) صاحب" منهاج الوصول إلى علم الأصول والله در الإمام (إمام الحرمين ) حيث قال في كتابه
Issue: Is obligation before the act or concurrent? It is attributed to al-Ash'ari that there is no obligation before the act, and this is necessarily false. How so? It entails denying the obligation of the disbeliever to believe and denying compliance, for compliance is by choosing the act after knowledge of the obligation. Nevertheless, a group followed him, including the author of al-Minhaj. May Allah have mercy on Imam al-Haramayn, who said: This is a doctrine that no rational person would accept for himself. In al-Ihkam: Obligation is established before the act and ceases by consensus. Is it remaining at the moment of occurrence? Al-Ash'ari held this, and it is false; because it is like saying that the demand remains when the demanded exists, which is as you see. And what is said: that obligation is attached to the whole, which occurs gradually, so it necessitates concurrence with occurrence, such that it is not completed in instants is false; because if the act is extended, the demand attached to it is analyzed into parts, each part preceded by a part of the demand. They said: The act is within power at that time because it is an effect of power, so obligation to it is valid, since there is no preventer except lack of power, and it is negated. We respond: We do not concede that it is an effect of power; for power has no effect according to you. And if conceded, we do not concede that it entails being within power; for it occurs by choice, because a thing does not exist unless it becomes necessary. And if conceded, we do not concede that there is no preventer except that; rather, it entails demanding what exists. And it is attributed to al-Ash'ari that there is no obligation before the act—that this attribution to al-Ash'ari is necessarily and obviously false. Then he noted its falsity and said: How could this attribution be false? Two things follow from this doctrine: First, on this assumption, the servant would not be obligated before accepting faith and performing it. Second, it entails negation of compliance, for compliance only occurs by choosing and performing the act after knowledge of the obligation and awareness of being obligated. And there is no obligation before the act for him to know it. (The error.) Then he pointed out the error of a group of scholars and said: Despite that (i.e., despite this attribution, a group of scholars followed al-Ash'ari), among them Imam al-Baydawi, author of Minhaj al-Wusul. May Allah have mercy on Imam al-Haramayn, where he said in his book...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute regarding the temporal relationship of taklif to the act. The view attributed to al-Ash'ari—that there is no obligation before the act—is presented and sharply refuted. The Hanafi (and majority) position is that taklif must precede the act; otherwise, the disbeliever would not be obligated to believe before actually believing, and compliance would be impossible since compliance requires knowledge of the obligation before acting. The author cites Imam al-Haramayn's strong rejection of this view. The text then delves into a more detailed argument: if taklif were concurrent with the act, it would involve demanding what already exists, which is absurd. For extended acts, the demand can be divided into parts, each part preceding the act part. The counter-argument that the act is within power as an effect of power is rebutted by denying that power has any effect (in the Ash'ari framework) and by arguing that the act must be necessitated by choice. The passage concludes by noting that despite the error, some scholars like al-Baydawi followed this attribution, but the author emphasizes the error. The lesson underscores the essential principle that taklif must logically and legally precede the act for meaningful obedience.

Key Terms
التكليف (al-taklif): The imposition of a legal obligation (command or prohibition) on the servant; it must exist before the act for valid compliance.
الامتثال (al-imtithal): Compliance: occurs by choosing and performing the act after knowledge of the obligation, requiring prior taklif.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi position insist that taklif must exist before the act?
A) Because power over the act requires prior knowledge of the obligation.
B) Because otherwise the disbeliever would not be obligated before believing, and compliance would be impossible.
C) Because the act is created by God and cannot be commanded.
D) Because taklif is only about mental commands.

Key Takeaway
Taklif must precede the act; otherwise, compliance is impossible and disbelievers would not be obligated.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
