Legal Capacity and Its Types: Minor's Faith as Case Study
Definition of legal capacity and application to minor's faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define legal capacity (ahliyyah), distinguish perfect from deficient capacity, and apply the Hanafi principle of secondary effects to validate a minor's purely good acts.

Core Idea
Legal capacity determines obligation vs. validity; purely good acts by minors are valid, and secondary harms do not invalidate.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi usul concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) — the quality that makes a human fit for legal rulings. The text defines capacity as twofold: perfect capacity entails obligation, deficient capacity allows valid performance without full liability. We then see a detailed application: a minor's faith is valid because it is purely good, and any apparent harm is secondary, not intended by the act. This reasoning illustrates how Hanafi usul uses istishab and talil to derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define ahliyyah and list its two types
Locate the definition in the text: capacity is suitability for acceptance and action regarding legal rulings. Identify the two types: perfect (kāmila) and deficient (qāṣira). Perfect requires complete intellect and body; deficient lacks one of them.
Perfect capacity is for the adult rational person; deficient applies to minors and the insane.
2
Distinguish the legal effects of perfect vs. deficient capacity
Perfect capacity imposes obligation of performance (wujūb al-adāʼ). Deficient capacity allows validity of performance (ṣiḥḥat al-adāʼ) without the obligation. Understand that validity does not depend on obligation.
A minor's act can be valid even if not obligatory.
3
Categorize acts under rights of Allah into three types
Acts are divided into purely good (ḥasan maḥḍ), purely evil (qabīḥ maḥḍ), and mixed. Faith is purely good. Under deficient capacity, purely good acts are valid from minors.
The text focuses on faith as the example of purely good.
4
Analyze the proof for validity of a minor's faith
The author uses istishab (absence of prohibition) and talil (faith is purely good). The Lawgiver would not prevent pure good. Any apparent harm (loss of inheritance, divorce) is attributed to the disbelief of others, not faith. Even if attributed to faith, it is secondary (tābiʿ), not intended (maqṣūd).
The analogy with emancipation from a gift of a relative slave reinforces that secondary effects do not invalidate the primary act.
5
Apply the rule to the opposite case (kufr)
The text indicates that a minor's disbelief would not be valid by qiyas, because it is purely evil. This shows consistency: purely evil acts are not valid from minors.
The passage is cut off, but the principle is clear.

تعريف الأهلية : هي كون الإنسان صالحاً للقبول و العمل بالأحكام الشرعية. اقسام الأهلية : وهي على نوعين : ١- كاملة بكمال العقل والبدن ، فيلزم على صاحب هذا النوع من الأهلية وجوب أداء ماكلف به . ٢ - وقاصرة بقصور أحدهما (قصور البدن أو العقل ) كالصبي العاقل ، وكالمعتوه البالغ ، والثابت معها (مع الأهلية القاصرة) صحة الأداء، أي صح من صاحب هذه الأهلية أداء الواجب ، فإنّ صحة نفس الوجوب تتعلق بالأهلية الكاملة ، وصحة وجوب الأداء تتعلق بالأهلية القاصرة. والتفصيل في ما يتعلق بالصبي صحة أدائه و مثله المعتوه أن مايكون مع الأهلية القاصرة صحة أدائه إما حق الله تعالى، وهو ثلاثة حسن محض، وقبيح محض، و بين الحسن و القبيح ، وإما حق العبد ، وهو أيضاً ثلاثة نافع محض، وضار محض، و دائر بينهما بين النافع و الضار). ۱ - مثال الأول من حقوق الله كالإيمان ، فانه حسن محض، لا يسقط حسنه (قط) وفيه نفع محض ، لأنه مناط (منشأ) سعادة الدارين ، فيصح الإيمان من الصبي. ولما كان توهم أن يقال : لعل الشرع لم يعتبر إيمان الصبي، دفع المصنف هذا التوهم وقال: والحجر (منع إيمان الصبي ) لم يوجد من الشارع، ولا يليق به (بالشارع) أن يمنع أحداً من الحسن المحض، والنافع الخالص، وسعادة الدارين. ثم أجاب المصنف عن سؤال مفروض، وهو أن الإيمان كيف يكون للصبي حسناً محضاً ونفعاً خالصاً ؟ وقد حرم عن ميراث قريبه الكافر، وعن زوجته المرتدة (الكافرة ) وهما بسبب الإيمان ، وهذا ضرر عظيم له ، فقال: وضرر الحرمان عن الميراث، وفرقة النكاح ليس لأجل الإيمان ، بل لأجل كفر القريب في الميراث، ولأجل كفر الزوجة وارتدادها في فرقة النكاح. فكفر القريب منع الميراث، و كفر الزوجة صار سبباً للتفريق، لأن الإيمان جالب الحقوق والكفر قاطعها هذا هو الجواب المنعى ، وأما لوسلم أن الحرمان والفرقة بسبب الإيمان فهو الحرمان أو الضرر في هذه الصورة بالتبع لا قصداً؛ لأن وضع الإيمان للنفع والسعادة ، و يتحقق بسبب الإيمان المخالفة مع أرباب الشقاوة ، فيترتب عليها على الشقاوة أحكامها تبعاً لوجود الإيمان، فهذا الضرر من لوازم الكفر دون الإيمان، وكم من شيئ حكيم يثبت تبعاً، لاقصداً، كترتب العتق على قبول الصبي هبة العبد القريب منه، أي اذا وهب لصبي عبد هو أبوه أو أخوه أو غيرهما من الأقارب ، فقبل الصبي الهبة يعتق عليه العبد، وإن كان في العتق له ضرر ، فليس هذا من لوازم الهبة ، بل من لوازم القرابة، فترتب العتق على الهبة، ليس قصداً، بل هو تبعاً ؛ لأن العتق من لوازم تملك القريب، سواء كان بالبيع أو بالإرث أو بالهبة. ۲ - والثاني من الحقوق المتعلقة بحقوق الله ، كالكفر فإنه ضد حق الله، وهو الإيمان ، فالكفر قطعه و تركه، والقياس يقتضي أن لا يصح كفر الصبي
Definition of legal capacity: it is that a human is fit for acceptance and action with regard to legal rulings. Types of capacity: it is of two types: 1) Perfect: by perfection of intellect and body, so upon the possessor of this type of capacity is the obligation of performing what he is charged with. 2) Deficient: by deficiency of one of them (deficiency of body or intellect), like the intelligent minor and the pubescent madman; and established with it (with deficient capacity) is validity of performance, i.e., the performance of the obligation is valid from the possessor of this capacity. For the validity of the obligation itself relates to perfect capacity, and the validity of the obligation of performance relates to deficient capacity. The detail concerning what relates to the validity of a minor's performance (and similarly the madman) is that what occurs with deficient capacity in terms of validity of performance is either a right of Allah Almighty, and it is three: purely good, purely evil, and between good and evil; or a right of the slave [of Allah], and it is also three: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and between beneficial and harmful. 1) Example of the first from rights of Allah is faith (iman), for it is purely good, its goodness never falls, and in it is pure benefit, because it is the source of happiness in both abodes, so a minor's faith is valid. Since it might be imagined that one could say: 'Perhaps the Lawgiver did not consider the faith of a minor valid,' the author repelled this imagination and said: 'The prohibition (preventing the minor's faith) is not found from the Lawgiver, nor is it befitting for Him to prevent anyone from pure goodness, pure benefit, and happiness of both abodes.' Then the author answered a hypothetical question: how can faith be purely good and purely beneficial for a minor when he is deprived of inheritance from his disbelieving relative and [separated] from his apostate wife, both due to faith, which is great harm for him. He said: 'The harm of deprivation from inheritance and separation of marriage is not because of faith, but because of the disbelief of the relative in inheritance, and because of the disbelief and apostasy of the wife in separation of marriage. For the disbelief of the relative prevented inheritance, and the disbelief of the wife became the cause of separation, because faith brings rights and disbelief severs them.' This is the answer that is approved. But if it is granted that deprivation and separation are due to faith, then the deprivation or harm in this case is secondary, not intended; because the purpose of faith is benefit and happiness, and due to faith there occurs opposition to the people of misery, so their rulings attach as a consequence of the existence of faith. So this harm is from the necessities of disbelief, not faith. And many a wise thing is established secondarily, not intentionally, like the effect of emancipation on a minor acceptance of a gift of a slave who is a relative: i.e., if a minor is gifted a slave who is his father, brother, or other relative, and the minor accepts the gift, the slave is emancipated upon him, even though there is harm in emancipation for him. This harm is not a necessary consequence of the gift, but of the kinship. So the emancipation's attachment to the gift is not intentional, but secondary. 2) The second from rights of Allah is disbelief (kufr), for it is opposite to the right of Allah (faith), so disbelief is cutting and abandoning, and analogy requires that a minor's disbelief be not valid.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an usul definition and expansion of the concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author defines capacity as suitability for receiving (rights) and performing (duties). He divides it into perfect capacity (ahliyyah kāmila) — requiring fulfillment of obligations — and deficient capacity (ahliyyah qāṣira) — allowing valid performance without full obligation. The focus then shifts to the validity of a minor's acts under deficient capacity. Acts are categorized into rights of Allah and rights of men, each subdivided into three types. The example of faith (iman) for Allah's rights shows that purely good acts are valid from the minor. The author rebuts the objection that faith causes harm (loss of inheritance and marriage), explaining that the harm is due to the relative's disbelief, not faith itself; even if it were due to faith, it is secondary and not intended. This is supported by an analogy: a minor's acceptance of a gift of a relative slave causes emancipation, which is a secondary effect of kinship, not the gift. Finally, it indicates that a minor's disbelief (kufr) would not be valid according to analogy, though the text is cut off.

Key Terms
الأهلية الكاملة (al-ahliyyah al-kāmila): Capacity that is complete due to fullness of intellect and body; it entails obligation of performance.
الأهلية القاصرة (al-ahliyyah al-qāṣira): Capacity that is deficient in one aspect (intellect or body); it allows valid performance but not full obligation.
حسن محض (ḥasan maḥḍ): An act that is purely good with no aspect of evil, like faith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the loss of inheritance and separation of marriage not considered a harm that invalidates a minor's faith?
A) Because these harms are caused by the minor's own disbelief.
B) Because these harms are secondary effects (bi al-tabaʿ) of faith, not intended by the Lawgiver.
C) Because the minor does not actually lose inheritance.
D) Because the minor's faith is not valid anyway.

Key Takeaway
A minor's faith is valid because it is purely good, and any secondary harm does not affect its validity.

The Validity of a Minor's Faith: A Purely Beneficial Right of Allah
Definition and defense of iman as purely beneficial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of rights of Allah and explain why a minor's faith is valid despite apparent incidental harms, using the qiyas of emancipation upon gift of a slave relative.

Core Idea
A minor's faith is pure good; incidental harms do not negate its essential benefit.

In this passage, the author categorizes rights of Allah into purely beneficial, purely harmful, and mixed. Faith (iman) is given as the prime example of a purely beneficial right. He then defends the validity of a minor's faith against the objection that it causes harm (loss of inheritance and marriage). The defense relies on two key points: those harms are due to disbelief, not faith, and even if they were due to faith, they are incidental, not intentional. An analogy from the law of emancipation upon a minor's acceptance of a gift of a relative slave supports this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorization of rights
Recognize that the passage begins by dividing rights of Allah and rights of the servant into three categories: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and mixed. Focus on the first category.
The author uses 'haqq Allah' as the frame for analyzing iman.
2
Locate the definition of iman as purely beneficial
Find the claim that iman is 'hasan mahd' (pure good) and 'nafi' mahd' (pure benefit) because it is the basis of happiness in both worlds.
This is the foundational claim that the author defends.
3
Understand the objection regarding apparent harms
The objection is that if iman were purely beneficial, then a minor who becomes Muslim would not suffer losses like being deprived of inheritance from a disbelieving relative or separation from a non-Muslim spouse.
These are real-world consequences that seem to contradict pure benefit.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author gives two lines of argument: (1) The harms are actually due to the disbelief of the other party, not to iman. (2) Even if the harms were directly linked to iman, they are incidental (taba'an), not intentional (qasdan).
The key distinction is between what the act is for (purpose) and what accompanies it (consequence).
5
Examine the qiyas (analogy) supporting the response
The author compares the incidental harm to the case of a minor who accepts a gift of a slave relative; the slave is emancipated even if that harms the minor. Emancipation is a known legal consequence of ownership of a relative, but it is not the purpose of the gift. Similarly, loss of inheritance and marriage are consequences of disbelief, not the purpose of iman.
This qiyas shows that incidental harms do not negate the essential benefit of the primary act.

...and between the good and the ugly, and as for the right of the servant, it is also three: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and between them, between beneficial and harmful. 1 - The first example from the rights of Allah is faith, for it is purely good, its goodness never falls away, and it contains pure benefit, because it is the source of happiness in both worlds, so faith from a minor is valid. When it was imagined that it might be said: perhaps the Lawgiver did not consider the faith of a minor, the author repelled this imagination and said: The prohibition (of the faith of a minor) has not come from the Lawgiver, and it is not befitting for the Wise Lawgiver to prevent anyone from pure good, pure benefit, and the happiness of both worlds. Then the author answered a presupposed question, which is: how can faith be purely good and purely beneficial for a minor when he is deprived of the inheritance of his disbelieving relative and of his apostate wife, both due to faith, and this is a great harm to him? He said: The harm of deprivation (of inheritance) and separation of marriage is not due to faith, but rather due to the disbelief of the relative in inheritance, and due to the disbelief and apostasy of the wife in the separation of marriage. So the disbelief of the relative prevented inheritance, and the disbelief of the wife became the cause of separation, because faith attracts rights and disbelief cuts them off. This is the intended answer. However, if one concedes that the deprivation and separation are due to faith, then the deprivation or harm in this case is incidental, not intentional, because the purpose of faith is benefit and happiness, and due to faith, opposition to the people of misery occurs, so their rulings follow incidentally from the existence of faith. So this harm is from the consequences of disbelief, not faith. There are many things that are established incidentally, not intentionally, like the occurrence of emancipation upon a minor's acceptance of a gift of a slave who is a relative, i.e., if a minor is given a slave who is his father, brother, or other relative, and the minor accepts the gift, the slave becomes free upon him, even if emancipation is harmful to him. This is not from the necessity of the gift, but from the necessity of kinship. So the occurrence of emancipation from the gift is not intentional but incidental, because emancipation is a consequence of ownership of a relative, whether by sale, inheritance, or gift.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the ruling that a minor's faith is valid. The author first categorizes rights into those purely beneficial, purely harmful, and mixed. Faith is the prime example of a purely beneficial right of Allah. The author addresses the objection that the apparent harm (loss of inheritance and marriage) contradicts pure benefit. He explains that the harm is actually due to the disbelief of the other party, not the faith itself. Even if the harm were directly from faith, it is incidental, not intentional. He supports this with an analogy: when a minor accepts a gift of a slave relative, the slave is emancipated, which may be harmful to the minor, but this emancipation is a consequence of kinship, not of the gift itself. Thus, the minor's faith is valid and beneficial despite any incidental losses.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): A right that belongs to Allah, either purely beneficial, purely harmful, or mixed.
نافع محض (nafi' mahd): Purely beneficial, with no harm intended or inherent.
صبي (sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty; his acts are considered but with varying legal effects.
الإيمان (al-iman): Faith; the act of believing in Allah and His Messenger; considered a purely good act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the loss of inheritance and marriage not considered a harm that negates the pure benefit of a minor's iman?
A) The minor's iman is not valid, so no loss occurs.
B) The harms are due to the disbelief of the other party and are incidental.
C) The minor can reclaim those rights by renouncing iman.
D) The harms are compensated by the Lawgiver in the afterworld.

Key Takeaway
A minor's iman is valid and purely beneficial; any worldly losses that accompany it are incidental and do not detract from its essential goodness.

The Validity of a Minor's Disbelief: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Usul dispute: istihsan vs. qiyas on a minor's disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a minor's kufr using istihsan and evaluate the opposing qiyas argument from al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Even a minor's disbelief matters in worldly rulings because its absolute prohibition overrides the lack of legal capacity.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute between qiyas and istihsan. While qiyas would suggest a minor's kufr has no effect due to their lack of capacity, the Hanafi school relies on istihsan to argue that the absolute prohibition of kufr makes it valid in worldly matters. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between apparent analogical reasoning and deeper juristic preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling by qiyas
Qiyas would treat a minor's kufr as pure harm and thus not valid. Note that al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf hold this view.
This is based on the principle that minors are not legally responsible.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its basis
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that a minor's kufr is valid in worldly matters by istihsan. What is the evidence they cite?
The proof is that kufr is absolutely prohibited (al-kufr mahzur mutlaqan).
3
Analyze the istihsan argument
The absolute prohibition does not depend on age or capacity. Therefore, the minor's kufr is considered despite the general rule that minors are not fully accountable.
This overrides the qiyas that would otherwise disregard the minor's act.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute affects rulings on inheritance and marriage: if a minor apostatizes, his marriage is dissolved and he loses inheritance rights according to the Hanafi school.
The Shafi'i school would not apply these consequences.
5
Distinguish worldly vs. afterlife rulings
In the afterlife, both schools agree that the minor's kufr is valid (e.g., no funeral prayer). Why the difference?
The difference is that worldly rulings can consider the absolute prohibition, while afterlife rulings are based on consensus and the minor's actual state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ - والثاني من الحقوق المتعلقة بحقوق الله ، كالكفر فإنه ضد حق الله، وهو الإيمان ، فالكفر قطعه و تركه، والقياس يقتضي أن لا يصح كفر الصبي (أن لا يعباً بكفره؛ لأنه ضرر محض ، وبه قال الإمام الشافعي والإمام أبو يوسف، لكن يصخ كفره (أي يعتبر كفره بدليل الاستحسان عندنا ( عند أبي حنيفة و محمد) (وهذا الخلاف في اعتبار كفر الصبي وعدمه في أحكام الدنيا من الميراث والنكاح وغيرهما) وأما في أحكام الآخرة فيصخ كفر الصبي ، أي يعتبر كفره اتفاقاً ، حتى لو مات الضبي الكافر لا يصلى عليه بالاتفاق. وجه الاستحسان ودليله) أن الكفر محظور (ممنوع) مطلقاً، سواء صدر من عاقل بالغ أو من صبى، فلا يسقط الكفر ولا يعفى بعذر غير مسموع ، وهو كونه محلاً للرحمة لأجل الصبا. فإن هذه الشقاوة الكاملة ( شقاوة الكفر) تخرجه عن كونه محلاً للرحمة، فلأجل اعتبار كفره تبين امرأته تطلق) طلاقاً بائناً ويُخرمُ عن ميراث اقاربه بالرّدة، وإنما لم يُقتل الصبي بالردة ( مع اعتبار ردّته ) بل قيد و حُبِسَ لأنه ( وجوب القتل) ليس بمجرد الارتداد، بل بالحرابة المحاربة مع الله ورسوله بعد الارتداد، وهو الصبي) ليس من أهل الحرابة ، ولا يقتل الصبي المرتد بعد البلوغ (أيضاً ) لأن في صحة صحته صحته إسلام الصبي خلافاً بين العلماء، قال بعضهم بصحة إسلامه و بعضهم بعدم صح فأورث الشبهة في جواز قتله.
2 - The second of the rights related to the rights of Allah is disbelief, for it is the opposite of the right of Allah, which is faith. So disbelief is its cutting and abandonment. Analogy requires that the disbelief of a minor is not valid (i.e., his disbelief is not considered) because it is pure harm. This is the position of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf. However, his disbelief is valid (i.e., his disbelief is considered) by the evidence of istihsan in our school (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). (This disagreement is regarding considering the minor's disbelief or not in worldly rulings like inheritance, marriage, etc.) As for the rulings of the afterlife, the minor's disbelief is valid, i.e., his disbelief is considered by consensus, such that if the disbelieving minor dies, funeral prayer is not performed for him by consensus. The basis and evidence of istihsan is that disbelief is absolutely prohibited, whether it comes from a sane adult or a minor. So disbelief is not dropped nor excused by an unacceptable excuse, which is being a subject of mercy due to childhood. Indeed, this complete misery (the misery of disbelief) removes him from being a subject of mercy. Therefore, due to considering his disbelief, his wife becomes separated (by a irrevocable divorce), and he is deprived of the inheritance of his relatives due to apostasy. However, the minor is not killed for apostasy (despite considering his apostasy), rather he is restrained and imprisoned because (the obligation of killing) is not merely due to apostasy, but due to hostility (muharaba) against Allah and His Messenger after apostasy, and the minor is not among the people of hostility. The apostate minor is not killed after puberty either, because regarding the validity of a minor's Islam there is disagreement among scholars; some hold it valid, some not, which creates doubt about the permissibility of killing him.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding the validity of a minor's kufr (disbelief) in worldly rulings. The default principle (qiyas) would suggest that a minor's kufr is not valid because it is pure harm and the minor is not fully responsible. This is the view of al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf. However, the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rules that a minor's kufr is valid in worldly matters by istihsan (juristic preference). The proof is that kufr is absolutely prohibited and its prohibition does not depend on the age of the perpetrator. Consequently, a minor's apostasy affects his marriage (irrevocable divorce) and inheritance (deprivation). However, unlike an adult apostate, the minor is not executed because execution is tied to muharaba (hostility), of which a minor is not capable, and there is a doubt (shubha) due to disagreement on the validity of a minor's Islam. In the afterlife, the minor's kufr is considered by consensus.

Key Terms
كفر (kufr): Disbelief; the opposite of faith; considered pure harm.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling.
ردة (riddah): Apostasy; the act of leaving Islam; has legal consequences like divorce and inheritance bar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), a minor's apostasy is considered valid in which domain?
A) In the afterlife only
B) In worldly rulings like inheritance and marriage
C) Only if the minor is a discerning minor
D) In neither domain

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to consider a minor's disbelief valid in worldly rulings, while the Shafi'i school relies on qiyas to deny it, illustrating the methodological difference between the two schools.

The Third Category of Divine Rights: Al-Da'ir Bayn al-Hasan wa al-Qabih
Defining the third category of divine rights and rulings for minors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the third category of rights of Allah (al-da'ir bayn al-hasan wa al-qabih), identify its locus (bodily acts of worship), and apply the ruling that a minor may perform them for reward without obligation to complete or expiate.

Core Idea
Acts good only at their specific times are valid from a minor for reward, but financial acts like zakat are not.

This passage introduces a key classification in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the middle category of acts that are good in their proper time but not otherwise. For a minor, these acts (prayer, fasting, Hajj) are valid for reward and habit formation, but the minor is not obligated to complete them or pay expiation. Understanding this distinction helps clarify the legal capacity of minors in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
What is the third category of rights of Allah? It is that which is between good and ugly with regard to time (al-da'ir bayn al-hasan wa al-qabih).
This category is defined by time-specificity.
2
Determine its locus
Which acts fall under this category? Bodily acts of worship: prayer, fasting, and Hajj.
These are physical acts (ibadat badaniyya).
3
Apply the ruling for minors
A minor may perform these acts for reward and habituation. If started, no obligation to complete or make up, and no expiation for violations (e.g., during Hajj).
The minor is not legally responsible (mukallaf).
4
Distinguish from financial acts
Unlike bodily acts, financial acts like zakat are not valid from a minor because they involve harm (giving wealth) and are not obligatory on the minor.
Zakat is a financial right (haqq mali).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۳- والثالث من حقوق الله، وهو الدائر بين الحسن والقبيح باعتبار الوقت كالصلاة وأخواتها من العبادات البدنية، كالصوم والحج ، فانها مشروعة في وقت أي وقتها المخصوص ) دون وقت كالصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة ، والصوم في أيام المنهية ، والحج في غير أشهره، يصح مباشرة الصبئ تلك العبادات، للثواب والاعتياد، بلا عهدة ضمان وقضاء عليه، فلايلزم عمل على الصبي بالشروع، فلو شرع في إحدى تلك العبادات (من الصلاة والصوم والحج ثم تركها لا يلزم عليه الإتمام أو القضاء، ولا يلزم على الصبي جزاء ارتكاب محظور الإحرام في الحج، بأن كان محرماً، فارتكب إحدى محظورات الإحرام لايلزم عليه جزاء ذلك المحظور، من الدم أو الصدقة أو النسك، بخلاف ماكان ذلك الحق مالياً من العبادات )، كالزكاة، فانها لاتصح منه ؛ لأن فيه (أداء الزكاة) ضرراً، مع عدم الوجوب عليه .
3 - The third of the rights of Allah is that which is between good and ugly with regard to time, like prayer and its counterparts from bodily acts of worship, such as fasting and Hajj. For they are legislated at a time (i.e., their specific time) but not at other times, like prayer at prohibited times, fasting on forbidden days, and Hajj outside its months. A minor may perform these acts of worship for reward and habituation, without liability for guarantee and making up. Thus no action is binding on the minor by starting; if he starts one of those acts of worship (prayer, fasting, Hajj) and then abandons it, he is not required to complete it or make it up, and there is no expiation on the minor for committing a prohibition of ihram during Hajj. If he is in ihram and commits one of the prohibitions of ihram, he is not liable for expiation for that prohibition, whether blood, charity, or sacrifice. This is different if that right is a financial act of worship, like zakat, for it is not valid from him because in it (paying zakat) there is harm, along with it not being obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage defines the third category of rights of Allah: acts that are good at their specific times but not otherwise, such as prayer (not at prohibited times), fasting (not on forbidden days), and Hajj (only in its months). A minor may perform these bodily acts of worship (e.g., prayer, fasting, Hajj) for reward and to accustom himself to worship. However, if he starts them, he is not obligated to complete them or make them up, and he is not liable for expiation for violations during Hajj (like breaking ihram). This is because the acts are not obligatory on him. In contrast, financial acts of worship like zakat are not valid from a minor because they involve harm (giving away wealth) without obligation. The ruling is based on the principle that the minor is not legally responsible for obligations but may perform supererogatory acts for reward.

Key Terms
الدائر بين الحسن والقبيح (al-da'ir bayn al-hasan wa al-qabih): An act that is good in its proper time but bad outside that time, like worship at forbidden times.
عبادات بدنية (ibadat badaniyya): Physical acts of worship such as prayer, fasting, and Hajj.
صبي (sabi): A minor; his acts of worship are valid for reward but not obligatory.
زكاة (zakat): Obligatory alms; a financial act of worship that is not valid from a minor because it involves harm and is not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acts is NOT valid from a minor according to the passage?
A) Performing prayer
B) Fasting in Ramadan
C) Giving zakat
D) Performing Hajj in its season

Key Takeaway
The third category of divine rights—acts that are good only at their specific times—are valid from a minor for reward, but the minor is not obligated to complete them or expiate violations, unlike financial acts like zakat.

Categories of Acts for Minors: Benefit, Harm, and Mixed
Defining three categories of rights for minors based on benefit and harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a minor's act as pure benefit, pure harm, or mixed benefit-harm, and apply the Hanafi rulings for each category, including the dispute on gross fraud.

Core Idea
A minor's act is valid based on its benefit-harm balance: pure benefit needs no guardian, pure harm is void, mixed requires guardian permission.

In Hanafi fiqh, the legal capacity of a minor is assessed by classifying his actions into three categories: those that are pure benefit, pure harm, or mixed. This classification determines whether the act requires guardian permission, is valid at all, or is prohibited. This lesson unpacks the criteria, with a focus on the intra-madhhab dispute over gross fraud transactions by an authorized minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the text, separate the categories: pure benefit (naf' mahd), pure harm (darar mahd), and mixed (dā'ir bayn al-naf' wa al-ḍarar). Note the examples given for each.
Pure benefit includes accepting gifts and deserving spoils; pure harm includes divorce and manumission; mixed includes sale and hire.
2
Determine the ruling for each category
For pure benefit, no guardian permission is needed; for pure harm, the act is invalid even with permission; for mixed, guardian permission is required.
The text explains the reasoning: benefit can be accepted without permission, harm is prevented absolutely, and mixed requires the guardian's judgment to offset potential harm.
3
Analyze the role of guardian permission
Understand how the guardian's permission compensates the minor's deficiency in the mixed category, making the authorized minor like an adult according to Abu Hanifa.
The concept of idhn (permission) is crucial; it transforms the minor's capacity in mixed transactions.
4
Evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute on gross fraud
The text presents a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the validity of a sale with gross fraud (ghubn fahish) by an authorized minor. Abu Hanifa allows it to strangers (in one narration also to the guardian), while the Sahibayn deny it entirely. The text prefers the Sahibayn's view.
The dispute hinges on the purpose of permission: to repel probable harm. A grossly fraudulent deal indicates the permission was misplaced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الرابع الذي هو الأول من حقوق العباد، ويكون نفعاً ، كقبول الهبة ، يصح مباشرة قبول الهبة من الصبي بلا إذن وليه؛ لأنه نفع محض في حقه ولذلك (أي لأجل أن العمل النافع يثبت في حق الصبي) من غير إذن الولى ، تجب أجرة الصبي المحجور الذى آجر نفسه لأحد، ثم فرغ من العمل مع بطلان عقد الإجازة في أول الأمر ولكن بشرط أن يكون الصبي حراً، واما العبد المحجور إذا آجر نفسه فيجب له بشرط السلامة بعد الفراغ من العمل، فلو هلك العبد في هذه الإجارة، فالقيمة واجبة على المستأجر، لا الآجر ، لأنّ المستأجر يصير غاصباً، فيضمن القيمة، واستحق الصبي الرضخ الأقل من سهم الغنيمة إذا حضر القتال مع عدم جواز شهوده وحضوره موضع القتل أي القتال بدون إذن الولى بالإجماع ؛ لأنّ عدم جواز شهود القتال والجهاد إنما كان لدفع ضرر الموت أو الخرج مع عدم وجوب الجهاد عليه فاما بعد الجهاد وهزيمة الأعداء، فأخذ الغنيمة أو الرضخ نفع محض ، فحكم بالاستحقاق . ٥- والخامس الذى هو ثانى حقوق العباد، وهو ضار محض كالطلاق ونحوه ، مثل الهبة و العتاق، فلا يملكه الصبي، ولو بإذن وليه، كما لا تملكه (أي النوع الخامس على الصبي غيره ( غير الصبي)، كالولى والقاضى ، فليست امرأته محلاً للطلاق قال شمس الأئمة السرخسى : زعم بعض مشايخنا أن هذا الحكم ، أي حكم طلاق امرأة الصبي غير مشروع أصلاً، حتى إن امرأته لا تكون محلاً للطلاق فصارت في هذا الحكم كالأجنبية في عدم جواز طلاقها و هذا وهم منه؛ فإن الطلاق يملك (يكون مملوكاً) بملك النكاح، فملك الطلاق من لوازم ملك النكاح، ولاضرر فيه (في ملك الطلاق) ، بل في عدم ملك الطلاق ضرر له باستيلاء الزوجة السوء عليه، وإنما هو (أي الضرر يكون) في الإيقاع ايقاع طلاق الصبي ، فلا يصح الإيقاع ، أما لو تحققت الحاجة (حاجة الصبي) إليه إلى إيقاع الطلاق لدفع الضرر كان الإيقاع صحيحاً. وإنما يجوز إقراض القاضى مال الصبي مع أن فيه ضرراً له؛ لأنه ليس يتبرع، بل هو حفظ لماله مع قدرة الاقتضاء، و طلب القرض من المستقرض بعلمه بخلاف الأب ، فإنه ليس له قدرة الاقتضاء إلا في رواية ، فان فيها (في تلك الرواية) جواز إقراض الأب مال الصبي لفور شفقته. ٦ - والسادس الذى هو الثالث من حقوق العباد، ودائر بين النفع والضرر، كالبيع والإجارة، وغيرهما من المعاوضات، ففيها نفع لاحتمال الربح ، مشوب ومخلوط باحتمال الضرر، كخسارة في ماله، فبانضمام رأى الولى مع عقل الصبي العاقل يندفع احتمال الضرر، فيملك الصبي هذه العقود مع رأى الولى وإذنه. ثم عند أبي حنيفة لما انجبر قصور الصبي (عقلاً) وبدناً برأى الولى وإذنه، كان الصبي المأذون كالبالغ في نفاذ تصرفاته ، فيملك مثل هذا الصبي البيع و الإجارة بغبن فاحش الغبن الفاحش هو ما لا يدخل تحته تقويم المقومين بأن زاد عن اصل القيمة أو الثمن زيادة على خلاف عرف التجار، أو حظ منها كذلك أي يملك البيع بغبن فاحش الأجانب باتفاق الروايات عن أبي حنيفة، وفي رواية أخرى عنه، لايملك الغبن الفاحش مع الولى؛ لأنّ الولى منهم في الأذن الجواز أن يكون حيلة للأخذ عن الصبي بأقل من قيمته، وعندهما أبي يوسف و محمد لا يجوز تصرف الصبي فيها دار بين النفع و الضرر بالغبن الفاحش قط، لا في الأجانب ولا في الولى، وقولهما (قول أبي يوسف و محمد) أظهر ؛ لأن اعتبار الإذن شرعاً لدفع الضرر المحتمل ، فلما عقد الصبي مع الغبن الفاحش عُلم أن إذن الولى لم يقع في محله.
The fourth, which is the first of the rights of people, and it is beneficial, like accepting a gift: it is valid for a minor to directly accept a gift without the permission of his guardian because it is pure benefit in his favor. Therefore (i.e., because a beneficial action is established in favor of the minor without the guardian's permission), the wage of a restricted minor who hires himself out to someone and then completes the work is obligatory, despite the invalidity of the hire contract initially, but on condition that the minor is free. As for a restricted slave, if he hires himself out, it is obligatory for him on condition of safety after completion of work; if the slave perishes during this hire, the compensation is due on the hirer, not the worker, because the hirer becomes a usurper and so guarantees the value. The minor deserves the lesser share (radhkh) of the spoils if he attends the battle, despite the impermissibility of his presence at the place of killing (i.e., the battle) without the guardian's permission by consensus; because the impermissibility of witnessing battle and jihad was only to repel the harm of death or injury, and jihad is not obligatory upon him. But after jihad and defeat of enemies, taking spoils or the lesser share is pure benefit, so entitlement is ruled. Fifth, which is the second of the rights of people, and it is purely harmful like divorce and similar, such as gift and manumission; the minor does not possess this, even with the permission of his guardian, nor does anyone else (i.e., the fifth type) possess it for the minor, like the guardian and the judge; thus his wife is not a locus for divorce. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi said: Some of our shaykhs claimed that this ruling, i.e., the ruling of divorce of the minor's wife, is not legislated at all, so that his wife is not a locus for divorce, and she becomes in this ruling like a foreign woman in the impermissibility of divorcing her. This is a misconception from him; for divorce is possessed by possessing the marriage contract; the ownership of divorce is among the necessities of ownership of marriage, and there is no harm in it (in the ownership of divorce), rather in the non-ownership of divorce there is harm to him from the domination of a bad wife. The harm is only in the execution (i.e., the minor executing divorce), so the execution is not valid. But if there is a need (the minor's need) to execute divorce to repel harm, then the execution is valid. It is permissible for the judge to lend the minor's property despite harm therein, because he is not donating but rather preserving his property with the ability to demand repayment, and the borrower's request with his knowledge is unlike the father; for the father does not have the ability to demand except in one narration, for in that narration is the permissibility of the father lending the minor's property due to his compassion. Sixth, which is the third of the rights of people, and it is between benefit and harm, like sale, hire, and other commutative contracts; in them there is benefit due to possibility of profit mixed with possibility of harm, like loss in his property. So with the addition of the guardian's opinion to the reasoning of the discerning minor, the possibility of harm is repelled, so the minor possesses these contracts with the guardian's opinion and permission. Then according to Abu Hanifa, since the minor's deficiency (in intellect and body) is compensated by the guardian's opinion and permission, the authorized minor is like an adult in the enforceability of his transactions; thus such a minor possesses sale and hire even with gross fraud (ghubn fahish) – gross fraud is that which does not fall within the evaluation of evaluators, i.e., increasing from the original value or price in a way contrary to the custom of merchants, or a share similarly – i.e., he possesses sale with gross fraud to strangers by agreement of narrations from Abu Hanifa. In another narration from him, he does not possess gross fraud with the guardian, because the guardian among them [might intend] in the permission a trick to take from the minor at less than value. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the minor's transaction in [matters] between benefit and harm with gross fraud is never valid, neither with strangers nor with the guardian. And their statement (the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is more apparent, because the consideration of permission legislatively is to repel probable harm; so when the minor contracts with gross fraud, it is known that the guardian's permission did not fall in its proper place.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the categories of acts for a minor (mahjoor) in Hanafi fiqh, specifically the fourth, fifth, and sixth categories of rights of people. The fourth category is pure benefit (naf' mahd), such as accepting a gift; the minor may directly accept without guardian permission, and even benefits like wages or spoils are valid. The fifth is pure harm (darar mahd), like divorce, manumission, or donating property; the minor cannot validly perform these even with guardian permission, and no one else can perform them on his behalf. The commentary critiques a claim by Sarakhsi that the minor's wife is not a locus for divorce at all; the correct view is that the minor does own the power of divorce as a consequence of marriage, but his actual pronouncement is invalid unless needed to repel harm (like a harmful wife). The sixth category is mixed benefit and harm (such as sale and hire), where the minor's act is valid only with guardian permission, and the guardian's judgment compensates for the minor's deficiency. Then an intra-madhhab dispute is presented: Abu Hanifa allows the authorized minor to sell or hire even with gross fraud (ghubn fahish) – i.e., selling far below market value – to strangers, but a narration restricts that with the guardian. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that such fraudulent transactions are never valid, because the guardian's permission is only to prevent harm, and a grossly unfair deal indicates the permission was misapplied; their view is deemed stronger.

Key Terms
نفع محض (naf' mahd): Pure benefit for the minor with no element of harm; such actions are valid without guardian permission.
ضرر محض (darar mahd): Pure harm with no benefit; such actions are invalid for the minor even with guardian permission, and cannot be performed by anyone else on his behalf.
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): Gross fraud: selling or buying at a price that deviates significantly from the true value, beyond the tolerance of evaluators.
صبي مأذون (sabi ma'dhūn): A discerning minor (saqīl) who has been given permission by his guardian to engage in trade. According to Abu Hanifa, such a minor is treated like an adult in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is invalid according to all Hanafi scholars.
B) The sale is valid according to Abu Hanifa but invalid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
C) The sale is valid only if the guardian is present.
D) The sale is nullified automatically after the minor matures.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a minor's act hinges on its category: pure benefit (valid without guardian), pure harm (void), mixed (requires guardian permission), with a dispute on gross fraud transactions among Hanafi imams.

Minor's Contracts with Excessive Fraud
Hanafi ruling on minor's contracts with excessive fraud when guardian permits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of the guardian's permission in authorizing a minor's contracts and apply the Hanafi preference in resolving the dispute regarding excessive fraud.

Core Idea
A minor's contract with excessive fraud is void per the preferred Hanafi view because it defeats the purpose of permission.

The Hanafi school recognizes that a discerning minor can engage in trade with the guardian's permission, but what happens when the minor is exploited? This passage from the sharḥ tradition presents a critical intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two Ṣāḥibayn over the validity of contracts involving excessive fraud (ghubn fāḥish). By dissecting the reasoning, you will learn how the 'illa (legal cause) of permission determines the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category of the contract
The text classifies sale and lease as contracts that lie between benefit and harm (dāʾir bayn al-nafʿ wa al-ḍarar). Recognize that such contracts require a balancing of potential profit and loss.
This categorization is crucial for understanding why the guardian's permission is needed.
2
Understand the role of guardian's permission
The guardian's opinion and permission compensate the minor's deficiency (jabr al-qusūr). This makes the authorized minor like an adult in transaction effectiveness.
The permission is not just a formality; it is a legal instrument to repel harm.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position on excessive fraud
According to Abu Hanifa, the authorized minor can sell even with excessive fraud (ghubn fāḥish) to strangers by agreed narrations; but a conflicting narration prohibits it with the guardian.
The exception for the guardian is based on preventing collusion.
4
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the minor's transaction with excessive fraud is absolutely invalid, whether with strangers or the guardian.
Their position is more stringent.
5
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
The text declares the Sahibayn's view as 'more apparent' (aẓhar) because the legal purpose of permission is to repel harm; excessive fraud shows the permission missed its mark.
This is a classic example of using the maqṣad (purpose) to resolve ikhtilāf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - والسادس الذى هو الثالث من حقوق العباد، ودائر بين النفع والضرر، كالبيع والإجارة، وغيرهما من المعاوضات، ففيها نفع لاحتمال الربح ، مشوب ومخلوط باحتمال الضرر، كخسارة، في ماله، فبانضمام رأى الولى مع عقل الصبي العاقل يندفع احتمال الضرر، فيملك الصبي هذه العقود مع رأى الولى وإذنه. ثم عند أبي حنيفة لما انجبر قصور الضبي ( عقلاً) و بدنا) برأى الولى وإذنه، كان الصبي المأذون كالبالغ في نفاذ تصرفاته ، فيملك مثل هذا الصبي البيع و الإجارة بغبن فاحش الغبن الفاحش هو ما لا يدخل تحة تقويم المقومين بأن زاد عن اصل القيمة أو الثمن زيادة على خلاف عرف التجار، أو حظ منها كذلك أي يملك البيع بغبن فاحش الأجانب باتفاق الروايات عن أبي حنيفة، وفي رواية أخرى عنه، لايملك الغبن الفاحش مع الولى؛ لأنّ الولى منهم في الأذن الجواز أن يكون حيلة للأخذ عن الصبي بأقل من قيمته، وعندهما أبي يوسف و محمد لا يجوز تصرف الصبي فيها دار بين النفع و الضرر بالغبن الفاحش قط، لا في الأجانب ولا في الولى ) ، وقولهما (قول أبي يوسف و محمد) أظهر ؛ لأن اعتبار الإذن شرعاً لدفع الضرر المحتمل ، فلما عقد الصبي مع الغبن الفاحش عُلم أن إذن الولى لم يقع في محله.
6 - And the sixth, which is the third of the rights of servants, and is intermediate between benefit and harm, like sale and lease and other commutative contracts. In them there is benefit due to the possibility of profit, mixed and blended with the possibility of harm, like loss in one's wealth. So with the addition of the guardian's opinion to the intellect of the discerning minor, the possibility of harm is repelled, so the minor owns these contracts with the guardian's opinion and permission. Then according to Abu Hanifa, since the minor's deficiency (in intellect and body) was compensated by the guardian's opinion and permission, the authorized minor is like an adult in the effectiveness of his transactions. So such a minor can undertake sale and lease even with excessive fraud. Excessive fraud is that which is not subject to approximation by appraisers, i.e., it exceeds the original value or price in a manner contrary to merchant custom, or its portion likewise. That is, he can sell with excessive fraud to strangers by agreement of the narrations from Abu Hanifa. In another narration from him, he cannot undertake excessive fraud with the guardian, because the guardian is among those in permission, and the permission could be a trick to take from the minor for less than its value. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the minor's transaction in that which is between benefit and harm is absolutely not valid with excessive fraud, neither with strangers nor with the guardian. And their statement (the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is more apparent, because the consideration of permission legally is to repel the potential harm. So when the minor contracts with excessive fraud, it is known that the guardian's permission was not given in its proper place.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal capacity of a discerning minor (ṣabī ʿāqil) who is authorized by his guardian to engage in commutative contracts. The text first categorizes such contracts as lying between benefit and harm (dāʾir bayn al-nafʿ wa al-ḍarar), meaning they carry a potential for both profit and loss. The guardian's participation is necessary to offset the risk of harm. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, once the guardian grants permission, the minor becomes like an adult in the effectiveness of his transactions. Therefore, the minor can sell even with excessive fraud (ghubn fāḥish) to strangers, by agreement of all narrations from Abū Ḥanīfa. However, there is a conflicting narration that he cannot do so with the guardian, as the permission might be a trick for the guardian to acquire the minor's property cheaply. Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad (the Ṣāḥibayn) hold that the minor's transaction is absolutely invalid when it involves excessive fraud, whether with strangers or the guardian. The text declares their view as more apparent (aẓhar) because the purpose of the guardian's permission is to ward off potential harm, and excessive fraud indicates the permission missed its mark.

Key Terms
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fāḥish): A discrepancy in price that cannot be approximated by appraisers, exceeding the original value contrary to merchant custom.
الصبي المأذون (al-ṣabī al-maʾdhūn): A discerning minor who has been given general permission by the guardian to engage in commercial transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on a minor's sale with excessive fraud when the guardian has given permission?
A) It is valid with strangers but not with the guardian.
B) It is valid with the guardian but not with strangers.
C) It is absolutely invalid in all cases.
D) It is valid in all cases without restriction.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view is that a minor's contract with excessive fraud is void because the guardian's permission is intended to repel harm, and excessive fraud contradicts that purpose.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

The Principle of No Hardship: Definition and Proof
Establishing the principle of no hardship and its proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the principle of no hardship (haraj) in religion, identify its Quranic proofs, and explain the Hanafi view that only legally recognized hardship (not rational) negates obligation.

Core Idea
Hardship is a graded term: only extreme, legally recognized difficulty relieves obligations, not mere rational difficulty.

This lesson introduces the fundamental usul principle that there is no hardship (haraj) in religion. The text establishes this principle from the Quran and clarifies that hardship is a graded term (mushakkak), meaning it applies in varying degrees. We will examine the evidence and a key disagreement with the Mu'tazila and Abu Zayd over the nature and scope of hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Extract the core statement 'لا حرج في الدين عقلاً ولا شرعاً' and note that it encompasses both rational and legal hardship, but only legal hardship is accepted by Hanafis.
The principle is derived from the Quranic verses cited later.
2
Analyze the term 'haraj'
Recognize that 'haraj' is a graded term (mushakkak) — its intensity varies across individuals. Understand that mitigation of rulings is proportional to the degree of hardship.
This explains why a completely incapable person has stronger exemption than a partially capable one.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
Read the three verses (22:78, 2:286, 65:7) and understand that they collectively negate any unbearable burden in religion. These are qati' al-thubut wa al-dalalah (definitive transmission and meaning).
The verses are the primary dalil for the principle.
4
Contrast Hanafi and Mu'tazila positions
The Mu'tazila argue that rational hardship (e.g., something rationally impossible) also negates obligation. Hanafis limit to legal hardship (shar'i), relying on the Quran and the practice of the Companions who undertook difficult acts.
The practical difference: e.g., a sick person may be exempted by Hanafis only if legally difficult, while Mu'tazila might exempt based on any rational difficulty.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference with Abu Zayd
Abu Zayd al-Dabusi held that a sane child and an adult with mental deficiency are still obligated for acts of worship as a precaution. The majority Hanafi view, based on the haraj principle, exempts them due to weakness of intellect and body.
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, the application of the principle can be contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة عدم الحرج فى الدين لا شرعاً ولا عقلاً: لا حرج (في الدين) عقلاً أو شرعاً، وهو مشكك فلهذا لم يجب شيء على الصبي العاقل ولا على المعتوه البالغ، خلافاً لأبي زيد، ولم يجب قضاء الصلاة في الحيض والنفاس دون الصوم، وشرعت العبادات في المرض قاعداً أو مضطجعاً، وانتفي الإثم في الخطأ مجتهداً، وفي النسيان، وسقط أكل الصائم، وخفف في السفر، فشرعت الرباعية ركعتين، ومسح الخف إلى ثلاثة أيام، وثبتت الرخصة بالشروع قبل تحققه، ولو أقام قبل المدة صح إقامته، ولزمت أحكام الإقامة، ولو في المفازة؛ لأنه دفع لها، وبعدها لا، إلا فيما يصح فيه؛ لأنه رفع بعد تحققه. (ما جعل الله عليكم في الدين من حرج) (لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها) (لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا ما آتاها) لا حرج (في الدين) عقلاً (كما يقول المعتزلة، فكلما فيه حرج عقلي لا يكون العبد مكلفاً به عندهم) ولا شرعاً، كما هو عندنا، والدليل الآيات المذكورة قبل كلام المصنف، وهو (الحرج) كلي مشكك أي يكون وجوده في أفراده متفاوتاً، فيصدق على بعض أفراده على سبيل الزيادة، وعلى بعضها على سبيل القلة والضعف، فإنّ العاجز من كل وجه حرجه أشد وأقوى من حرج العاجز من بعض الوجوه، فتخفيف الأحكام يكون بقدر الحرج في كل ذي حرج، فلهذا أي لأجل عدم الحرج في الدين شرعاً لم يجب شيء على الصبي العاقل، ولا على المعتوه البالغ لضعف العقل والبدن في الأول ولضعف العقل فقط في الثاني. خلافاً لأبي زيد الدبوسي، فإنه قال في "التقويم" إنا لا نسقط وجوب العبادات لأجل العته احتياطاً. 2- ولأجل رعاية الحرج لم يجب قضاء الصلاة في حال الحيض والنفاس دون الصوم، أي يجب قضاء الصوم على الحائض والنفساء بعد طهرهما. 3- ومن أجل ذلك شرعت العبادات (الصلوات) في المرض قاعداً أو مضطجعاً (أو إيماء). 4- وانتفى الإثم في الخطأ اجتهاداً (حال كونه مجتهداً) كما في القبلة. 5- وانتفى الإثم في النسيان. 6- وسقط أكل الصائم نسياناً. 7- وخفف في السفر، فشرعت الرباعية ركعتين. 8- وزيدت مدة المسح في السفر إلى ثلاثة أيام وليالها. 9- وثبتت الرخصة بالشروع في السفر قبل تحققه ووصول المسافر إلى نهاية السفر، وتمام مدة السفر إلى ثلاثة أيام، ولو أقام المسافر (نوى الإقامة) قبل المدة (قبل مدة السفر) صح إقامته، ولزمته أحكام الإقامة من التمام، والمسح إلى يوم وليلة وغيرهما، ولو كانت إقامته في المفازة التي لا بنيان فيها، مع أن المفازة ليس بمحل الإقامة؛ لأنه (صيرورته مقيماً) قبل المدة رفع للسفر قبل تحققه، ورفع السفر بنية الإقامة رفع للرخصة التي كان سببها السفر، وبعدها (بعد مدة السفر) لا يصح كونه مقيماً إلا فيها (في موضع) يصح فيه كونه مقيماً من مصر أو قرية؛ لأنه (كونه مقيماً بعد مدة السفر) رفع للسفر بعد تحققه، وتجديد الإقامة.
Issue: There is no hardship in religion, neither legally nor rationally: there is no hardship (in religion) rationally or legally, and it is a graded term. For this reason, nothing is obligatory upon a sane child nor upon an adult with mental deficiency, contrary to Abu Zayd. And the makeup of prayer during menstruation and postnatal bleeding is not obligatory, unlike fasting. And acts of worship are prescribed for the sick sitting or lying down. And sin is negated in error when making ijtihad, and in forgetfulness. And the eating of a fasting person (forgetfully) is excused. And concessions are given in travel: the four-rak'ah prayer is prescribed as two rak'ahs, and wiping over the khuff is extended to three days. And the concession is established by starting the journey before its actual occurrence. And if he intends to reside before the period (of travel), his residence is valid and the rulings of residence become binding, even if in a desert where there is no building; because it is a repulsion of concession before it is realized, and after the period it is not valid except in a place where residence is valid, because it is a removal after realization. (Allah has not placed upon you in religion any hardship) (Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity) (Allah does not burden a soul except with what He has given it). There is no hardship (in religion) rationally (as the Mu'tazila say, for whatever has rational hardship, the servant is not obligated by it according to them) nor legally, as it is with us. The proof is the verses mentioned before the author's speech. And (hardship) is a generic term that admits degrees, meaning its existence in its individuals is varying; it applies to some individuals by way of increase, and to some by way of decrease and weakness. Indeed, the one who is completely incapable, his hardship is more severe and stronger than the hardship of one incapable in some respects. So the mitigation of rulings is according to the degree of hardship in each case. For this reason, i.e., due to the absence of hardship in religion legally, nothing is obligatory upon a sane child nor upon an adult with mental deficiency due to weakness of intellect and body in the former and weakness of intellect alone in the latter. Contrary to Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, for he said in al-Taqwim: 'We do not drop the obligation of acts of worship due to mental deficiency as a precaution.' 2- And for the sake of observing hardship, the makeup of prayer during menstruation and postnatal bleeding is not obligatory, unlike fasting, i.e., it is obligatory for the menstruating and postnatal woman to make up fasting after purification. 3- And for this reason, acts of worship (prayers) are prescribed for the sick sitting or lying down (or by gestures). 4- And sin is negated in error in ijtihad (when being a mujtahid) as in the case of the qibla. 5- And sin is negated in forgetfulness. 6- And the eating of a fasting person (forgetfully) is excused. 7- And concessions are given in travel, so the four-rak'ah prayer is prescribed as two rak'ahs. 8- And the period of wiping over the khuff in travel is increased to three days and nights. 9- And the concession is established by starting the journey before its actual occurrence and before the traveler reaches the end of the journey, and the completion of the journey period up to three days. And if the traveler intends to reside (intends residence) before the period (before the period of travel), his residence is valid, and the rulings of residence become binding on him, such as completing the prayer and wiping over the khuff for one day and night and others, even if his residence is in a desert where there is no building, despite the desert not being a place of residence; because his becoming a resident before the period is a removal of travel before it is realized, and the removal of travel by intention of residence is a removal of the concession whose cause was travel. And after it (after the travel period), it is not valid for him to become a resident except in a place where it is valid for him to become a resident, like a city or village; because his becoming a resident after the travel period is a removal of travel after its realization and a renewal of residence.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion of the usul principle that there is no hardship (haraj) in religion, both rationally and legally. The author begins by stating that hardship is a graded term (mushakkak), meaning it applies in varying degrees. This principle is derived from several Quranic verses (22:78, 2:286, 65:7). The author then lists numerous applications (furu') that demonstrate how this principle mitigates rulings: (1) no obligation on a sane child or an adult with mental deficiency – a point disputed by Abu Zayd al-Dabusi; (2) no makeup of prayer during menstruation and postnatal bleeding, but fasting must be made up; (3) sick people may pray sitting or lying down; (4) no sin for error in ijtihad (e.g., facing wrong qibla); (5) no sin for forgetfulness; (6) eating forgetfully while fasting does not break the fast; (7) travel allows shortening four-rak'ah prayers to two; (8) wiping over khuff is extended to three days in travel; (9) concessions begin with starting the journey, not just after traveling a certain distance. An important sub-issue discusses the intention to reside (niyyat al-iqama) during travel: if one intends to reside before completing the travel period (three days), the travel concession ends and full rulings apply, even if in a desert; but after the period, the intention to reside is only valid in a proper dwelling place. The text also highlights a key disagreement with the Mu'tazila: they hold that rational hardship negates obligation, while the Hanafi position is that only legal (shar'i) hardship does so, based on the Quranic proofs.

Key Terms
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship or difficulty; a generic term that admits varying degrees (mushakkak).
مشكك (mushakkak): A term that applies to its individuals with varying intensity (e.g., hardship can be more or less).
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession granted due to hardship, such as shortening prayer in travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, what is the correct understanding of 'no hardship in religion'?
A) Any difficulty, whether rational or legal, exempts a person from obligation.
B) Only legally recognized extreme hardship can mitigate or remove an obligation.
C) Hardship is purely subjective, so each person decides their own exemptions.
D) The principle only applies to acts of worship, not to transactions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of no hardship (haraj) is grounded in the Quran and applies only to legally recognized extreme difficulty, not merely rational difficulty.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Applications of the No-Hardship Principle
Listing and explaining applications of the no-hardship principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the main applications of the no-hardship principle mentioned in the source and explain how each reflects proportional mitigation according to the degree of hardship.

Core Idea
From exemption of the insane to travel concessions, the haraj principle shapes rulings across fiqh, each mitigation proportional to the severity of hardship.

Building on the principle of no hardship, this lesson explores its concrete applications in Islamic law. The source text enumerates nine key rulings—from the exemption of the insane from obligations to the details of travel concessions—each demonstrating how the law alleviates hardship in proportion to its intensity.

Guided Path
1
Exemption of the insane and child
The first application: nothing is obligatory upon a sane child or an adult with mental deficiency. The reason is weakness of intellect and body, which constitutes extreme hardship.
This is a direct application of the principle: the hardship is so great that the obligation is dropped entirely.
2
Menstruation and postnatal bleeding
The makeup of prayer during menstruation and postnatal bleeding is not obligatory, but the makeup of fasting is. The difference is that repeating many prayers (over several days) is excessively burdensome, while fasting involves only a few days.
This shows the graded nature of hardship: the hardship of making up prayers is greater, so it is dropped; the hardship of making up fasting is lesser, so it remains.
3
Sickness and prayer
The sick are allowed to pray sitting, lying down, or by gestures. This is a mitigation due to the physical hardship of standing and bowing.
The hardship here is partial, so the obligation is mitigated, not dropped.
4
Error in ijtihad and forgetfulness
Sin is negated in error when making ijtihad (e.g., facing the wrong qibla) and in forgetfulness. Also, a fasting person who eats forgetfully does not break the fast.
These are cases of unavoidable hardship—lack of intention or awareness—so the ruling is relaxed.
5
Travel concessions
In travel, the four-rak'ah prayer is shortened to two, and wiping over khuff is extended to three days. These are concessions because travel naturally involves hardship.
The concession begins with starting the journey, not just after a certain distance.
6
Intention to reside during travel
If a traveler intends to reside before completing the travel period (three days), the travel concession ends and full rulings apply, even in a desert. After the period, residence is only valid in a proper dwelling.
This sub-rule ensures that the concession is not abused: once the traveler settles, the hardship of travel ends.

This passage is a comprehensive discussion of the usul principle that there is no hardship (haraj) in religion, both rationally and legally. The author begins by stating that hardship is a graded term (mushakkak), meaning it applies in varying degrees. This principle is derived from several Quranic verses (22:78, 2:286, 65:7). The author then lists numerous applications (furu') that demonstrate how this principle mitigates rulings: (1) no obligation on a sane child or an adult with mental deficiency – a point disputed by Abu Zayd al-Dabusi; (2) no makeup of prayer during menstruation and postnatal bleeding, but fasting must be made up; (3) sick people may pray sitting or lying down; (4) no sin for error in ijtihad (e.g., facing wrong qibla); (5) no sin for forgetfulness; (6) eating forgetfully while fasting does not break the fast; (7) travel allows shortening four-rak'ah prayers to two; (8) wiping over khuff is extended to three days in travel; (9) concessions begin with starting the journey, not just after traveling a certain distance. An important sub-issue discusses the intention to reside (niyyat al-iqama) during travel: if one intends to reside before completing the travel period (three days), the travel concession ends and full rulings apply, even if in a desert; but after the period, the intention to reside is only valid in a proper dwelling place. The text also highlights a key disagreement with the Mu'tazila: they hold that rational hardship negates obligation, while the Hanafi position is that only legal (shar'i) hardship does so, based on the Quranic proofs.

Key Terms
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship or difficulty; a generic term that admits varying degrees (mushakkak).
مشكك (mushakkak): A term that applies to its individuals with varying intensity (e.g., hardship can be more or less).
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession granted due to hardship, such as shortening prayer in travel.

Key Takeaway
The no-hardship principle is operationalized in numerous specific rulings, each tailored to the degree of hardship involved.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Death as a Destroyer of Obligation and the Ruling on Kafala After Death
Explains that death abrogates taklif and presents the ikhtilaf on kafala after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that death destroys taklif to identify which obligations survive and evaluate the dispute on kafala after death.

Core Idea
Death destroys the basis of legal obligation, but obligations attached to property remain for settlement.

In this passage, we explore a foundational usul principle: death ends the basis of taklif (legal obligation). But what happens to debts and other obligations? The text clarifies that some obligations survive, while others vanish. A key application is the dispute over suretyship (kafala) for the deceased—a disagreement within the Hanafi school itself. By understanding the reasoning, you will grasp how usul principles shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle: death destroys taklif
Read the opening statement: 'Death is the destroyer of the basis of obligation.' Understand that this means legal accountability ends, but certain obligations remain tied to property or specific objects.
The principle is universal, but the exceptions (debt, bequests, funeral expenses) are critical.
2
List the surviving obligations
From the text, enumerate what remains on the deceased's dhimma: obligations attached to a specific 'ayn (deposits, usurped goods) and obligations attached to property (debts, bequests, funeral expenses). Note the priority: funeral expenses first.
3
Analyze the dispute on kafala after death
The passage presents Abu Hanifa's view (invalid) and the Sahibayn's view (valid). Identify their respective proofs: Abu Hanifa uses the logical requirement of a claim (mutalaba), while the Sahibayn rely on the hadith of Jabir.
Also note that the other three imams agree with the Sahibayn.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the hadith
The author provides counter-interpretations: the hadith may indicate a promise (i'tida') rather than kafala, or it may be an acknowledgment of a prior kafala. Understand that this undercuts the proof for the Sahibayn.
This step illustrates how usul principles (e.g., interpreting ambiguous evidence) affect ruling derivation.
5
Apply the principle to understand voluntary payment (tabarru')
The text states that voluntary payment of the deceased's debt is valid. This is consistent with the principle: the debt remains from the creditor's perspective, even though the debtor's obligation has fallen due to death.
Distinguish between the survival of the debt as a financial right and the fall of the duty to pay from the deceased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة أن الموت هادم لأساس التكليف
الموت هادم لأساس التكليف، فلا يبقى على ذمة الميت إلا ما كان متعلقاً بعين كالودائع والغصوب أو بمال تركه كالديون والوصايا والتجهيز، ويقدم التجهيز على الديون والوصايا بالإجماع. فلا تصح الكفالة بما عليه بعد الموت عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الكفالة ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في المطالبة ولا مطالبة، فلا ضم. وعندهما (أبي يوسف ومحمد) تصح الكفالة عن الميت، وبه قالت الأئمة الثلاثة، لحديث جابر: إن أبا قتادة قال: هما (الديناران) على يا رسول الله، فصلى عليه، فكفل أبو قتادة عن الميت. ولأن الموت لا يبرئ الميت عن الدين، ولذا (لعدم برائته) يطالب بالدين في الآخرة إجماعاً. ويصح التبرع للميت بالأداء عنه، أي بأداء الغير دينه عنه. والجواب عنه أنه يحتمل عدة الأداء لا الكفالة، ويحتمل أن يكون إقراراً بكفالة سابقة، وفيه ما فيه. والمطالبة الأخروية باعتبار الإثم لا تفتقر إلى بقاء الذمة. وصحة التبرع لبقاء الدين من جهة من له الدين، فإن سقوط الأداء بالموت لضرورة فوت المحل، فيظهر هذا السقوط في حق من عليه الدين دون حق من له الدين، فيبقى الدين من جهة من له.
Issue: Death is the destroyer of the basis of obligation. Death destroys the basis of obligation, so nothing remains on the liability of the deceased except what is attached to a specific object (like deposits and usurped property) or attached to his property (like debts, bequests, and funeral expenses), and funeral expenses are given priority over debts and bequests by consensus. So suretyship (kafala) for what is upon him after death is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, because suretyship is attaching the liability to the liability in claim, and there is no claim (after death), so there is no attachment. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), suretyship for the deceased is valid, and the three imams said this, due to the hadith of Jabir: that Abu Qatada said: 'Two dinars are upon me, O Messenger of Allah,' so he prayed over him, and Abu Qatada became surety for the deceased. And because death does not exempt the deceased from debt, and therefore (due to his non-exemption) he is demanded for the debt in the Hereafter by consensus. And voluntary discharge (tabarru') for the deceased by paying off his debt is valid, i.e., another person paying his debt. The response to it is that it (the hadith) may imply a promise of payment, not suretyship, and may be an acknowledgment of a prior suretyship, and it is questionable. And the Hereafter claim is based on sin, not dependent on the survival of the liability. And the validity of voluntary discharge is because the debt remains from the side of the creditor, for the fall of payment due to death is by necessity of the loss of the location (of the act), so this fall appears in the right of the debtor, not in the right of the creditor, so the debt remains from the side of the creditor.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage addresses a fundamental usul principle: death abrogates the basis of taklif (legal obligation). The author states that upon death, only obligations that are attached to a specific 'ayn (like deposits or usurped goods) or to the deceased's property (like debts, bequests, and funeral expenses) remain on his dhimma. Funeral expenses are given priority over debts and bequests by consensus. The main issue discussed is the validity of kafala (suretyship) for the deceased's debts after death. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such kafala is invalid because kafala involves adding the surety's liability to the principal's liability for the purpose of claim, and after death there is no worldly claim, so there is no basis for adding liability. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that kafala after death is valid, agreeing with the other three imams. They cite the hadith of Jabir where Abu Qatada said he would pay the two dinars of the deceased, and the Prophet prayed over him. They also argue that death does not exempt the deceased from the debt, as he will be held accountable in the Hereafter. The author responds to this evidence by suggesting that Abu Qatada's statement may have been a promise to pay rather than a kafala, or an acknowledgment of a prior kafala. The claim in the Hereafter is based on sin, not the survival of the dhimma. The validity of voluntary payment (tabarru') for the deceased is accepted because the debt remains valid from the creditor's perspective; the payment obligation falls due to the impossibility of performance after death, but this fall applies only to the debtor's side, not the creditor's. This aligns with the Hanafi position that kafala after death is not valid, but voluntary payment is commendable.

Key Terms
الموت (al-mawt): Death; according to this text, it is hādim li-asās al-taklīf (destroyer of the basis of obligation), meaning it terminates legal capacity for obligations.
التكليف (al-taklīf): Legal obligation; the address of the Lawgiver concerning an act that is either commanded or prohibited, addressed to a legally capable person.
الكفالة (al-kafāla): Suretyship; a contract where the surety (kafil) adds his liability to that of the principal for the purpose of claiming payment.
الذمة (al-dhimma): Legal liability or capacity; the attribute by which a person is capable of bearing obligations and rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what hadith was used as evidence by the Sahibayn for the validity of kafala after death?
A) Hadith of Abu Qatada about two dinars
B) Hadith of Jabir about Abu Qatada becoming surety
C) Hadith of Aisha about the deceased's debts
D) Hadith of Ibn Abbas about funeral expenses

Key Takeaway
Death terminates the basis of legal obligation, but obligations attached to property survive for settlement; the validity of kafala after death is disputed within the Hanafi school based on the interpretation of the hadith of Jabir.
